Geodesy\n\nArtificial\nsatellites\nin close-to-Earth\norbits\nhave contributed\nto the field of geodesy\na\nnew technique\nwhich, theoretically\nspeaking,\nis capable\nof completely\nreorienting\nthe\nmethods and procedures\nof the geodetic discipline.\nApplication\nof newly\ndeveloped\nmethods of precision\nmeasurements\nin satellite triangulation\nconfirm\nearly predictions\nof a reformation\nin the domain of classical\ngeodetic field operations\n(Schmid, 1966a).\nWithout entering\nhere into questions\nconcerning\nthe dividing\nline between\ngeodesy\n_.u s=vp-_l_,\nit can be _a_ud that the\nfundamental\nproblem of geodesy is the mathematical\ndescription\nof the Earth\'s\ngravity\nfield together\nwith the determination\nof the\ngeometry\nof the physical\nsurface\nof the\nEarth,\nwith\nunambiguous\ncorrespondence\nbetween the Earth-fixed\ncoordinate\nsystems\nor datums\nand the spherical\ncoordinate\nsystern for a given epoch that serves as reference\nframe for metric astronomy.\nWith satellite\ngeodesy it is possible to find a solution to the\nfundamental\nproblem\non a synoptical\nbasis,\ni.e., with reference\nto the whole Earth.\nFurthermore,\ntriangulation\nwith satellites,\nin\nconjunction\nwith position\nand time determinations\nof satellite\norbits, eventually\nprovides the necessary\nlink between\nthe geometric and geophysical\nmeasuring\nconcepts\nof geodesy.\nThus, with the aid of satellite\ngeodesy it\nbecomes possible to undertake\nthe geometric\ndescription\nof the surface and the analytical\ndescription\nof the gravity\nfield of the earth\nby means of worldwide\nmeasuring\nsystems\nand to derive results\nin the form of threedimensional\nmodels based on a minimum\nof a\npriori hypotheses.\n\n527\n\nSURVEY\n\nThese mathematical\nmodels then represent\nthe frame of reference\ninto which one can\nfit the existing\ngeodetic\nresults\nfrom\nthe\nvarious local datums,\nas well as all geodetic\nmeasurements\nto be executed\nin the future.\nThe relevant\nnecessary\nadjustment\nshould\nnot confine itself to the limited, in practice,\nclassical\nconcept\nof the treatment\nof accidental errors,\nbut must, with the aid of a\ngeneralization\nof the Gaussian\nalgorithm,\ntake advantage\nof the increasing\nknowledge\nderived\nfrom\ninterdisciplinary\nresearch\nsources concerning\nthe various\ngeophysical\nparameters\ninvolved, with a meaningful\ninclusion\nof the\ncorresponding\ncovariance\nmatrices.\nFrom a formalistic\nmathematical\npoint of\nview, the significance\nof artificial\nsatellites\nfor geodesy consists of the ability to express\nthe .....\nume-pos_uon\ncurve\nof the orbit of a\nclose-to-Earth\nsatellite in terms of development,\nthe\nquantities\ndescribing\nthe gravitational\nfield\nare of prime importance\nto gravimetry;\nthe\nremainder\nof the geophysical\nforces affecting\nthe orbit or arising\nfrom the satellite\nitself\nare treated as perturbation\nsources.\nThe quantitative\ndetermination\nof the\nparameters\nappearing\nin the mathematical\nsimulation\nof the satellite\norbits is accomplished by setting\nup observation\nequations\nwhich functionally\nrelate the measurements\nmade for the orbit determination\nwith the\nparameters\ndescribing\nthe orbit.\nIt is then\napparent\nthat, in addition\nto these orbital\nparameters,\nthese equations\nwill involve the\nposition\ncoordinates\nof the Earth-fixed\nobservation\nstations\nwhich, in the geophysical\ncontent of the problem, represent\nthe position\nof these stations relative to the Earth\'s\nmass\ncenter.\nIf there is a sufficiently large number\nof observations,\noptimally\ndistributed,\nit is\npossible to world-\n\nThis, from a purely\ntheoretical\nstandpoint,\nattractive\ntrain\nof thought\nhas found\ngreat\nappeal,\namong\nastronomers\nand geophysicists\nin particular,\nand has already\nled to impressive results\nand new insights\n(Kozai,\n1966a).\nBeing\nmore\nintimately\nconnected\nwith\ntriangulation\nmeasurements\nproper,\nhowever,\nthe measurement\nengineer\nand, in orbital\nelements.\n\nis\nthe\nsta-\n\nGiven the results\nof the geometric\nsolution,\nthe opportunity\npresents\nitself,\nby way of a\npurely\ngeometric\norbit\ndetermination,\nto ascertain\nthe information\ncontent of the dynamic solution is not completely\nexhausted,\ncan be\ncountered\nby seeing the eventual\nsolution of\nthe problem of satellite geodesy as a combination of the separate, The amalgamation\nof the outputs\nof geometric\nand dynamic\nsatellite\ngeodesy\nmust in the end be consummated,\nfrom the\ntheoretical\nas well as the practical\nstandpoint, by the inclusion of geuueuc uuL_t meas..........\nured on the surface\nof the Earth.\nThis requirement\nseems necessary\nbecause, although\nthe significant\ncontribution\nof satellite geodesy to physical geodesy has been to open up\nthe third dimension\nin the investigation\nof\nthe Earth\'s\ngravity\nfield, the fact still remains that the essential\ntasks of geodesy are\nthe determination\nof the geometry\nof the\nphysical surface of the Earth and the representation\nof the gravity field in detail and relatively close to the crust (Kaula, 1967a).\n7.1.2\n\nDevelopment and Organization of a Geodetic Satellite Program for Creating a\nWorldwide Geodetic Reference System\n\nThe history\nof satellite\ngeodesy\nand its\ntheoretical\ndevelopment\nbegan with the implementation\nof an idea that had been for\ndecades an intermediary\ngoal for scientists\nconcerned\nwith rocket development:\nto increase the cutoff velocity of the rocket to the\npoint where\nit goes into orbit around\nthe\nr.artn.\n\nSURVEY\n\n529\n\nThe realization\nof this technical\ngoal with\nthe launch of the first Russian\nand, shortly\nthereafter,\nof the first American\nartificial\nsatellite created renewed\ninterest\namong experts in the fields of astronomy\nand aeronomy\nin the theoretical\nproblems\nconcerned\nwith\nthe description\nof the track of a body of small\nweight orbiting\naround an oblate mass, specifically around the Earth.\nThe classical theories and procedures\nof physical geodesy being\ninadequate\nto the solution of all these problems, it has become the practice\nto apply almost exclusively\nthe classical\nprinciples\nof\ncelestial\nmechanics\ntogether\nwith theories\nand results from the fields of aeronomy\nand\nrelated\ngeosciences,\nwhich with the aid of\nrocket experiments\nhave already\nmade considerable\nadvances\nin their studies\non the\nsubject.\nThis development\nexplains the dominating\ninfluence of dynamic satellite geodesy to this\nday, reflected also in the planning\nand execution of the first American\ngeodetic\nsatellite\nprogram. The basic requirement\nfor the satellite launched\nin the first American\ngeodetic\nsubsequent\nGEOS satellite program\nwas compact construction\nand rotation-symmetric\nform to the highest possible degree.\nThe resulting mass to cross-section\nratio was designed to minimize\nthe perturbing\ninfluence\nof the atmosphere\nand other\ngeophysical\nforces, such as solar radiation\npressure,\nin order not to complicate\nunnecessarily\nthe adjustment\nof the orbit relative to the gravitational field.\nIn order to be able to sense the essential\ncomponents\nof the Earth\'s\ngravitational\nfield\nwhile keeping\nperturbing\ninfluences\nwithin\nbounds, a problem intimately\nconnected\nwith\nthat of assuring\nthe satellite\na sufficiently\nlong lifetime, the necessary experiments\nwere\nexecuted at heights of 1000 to 1600 km above\nthe Earth, and the nearly circular orbits were\ndistributed\nover as wide a band of inclination\nas possible.\nThe equipment\nfor this type of\nsatellite was characteristic\nof its purpose, the\ninstruments\non the satellite\nallowing,\nwhen\noperated\ntogether\nwith instruments\non the\n\n530\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nlite distance and direction,\nand the difference\nin distance to the satellite at two points in its\norbit (or equivalently,\nthe frequency\nof radio\nwaves received from the satellite;\nsee chs. 2\nand 5 and sec. 7.2 of this chapter).\nIt is apparent\nthat, viewed in the light of\nthe present\nstate of development,\ndynamic\nsatellite\ngeodesy in general\nfaces two complexes of questions\nrequiring\nfurther\nstudy\nin the planning\nfor future\ngeodetic\nsatellite\nprojects.\nFrom the theoretical\nside, for one,\nthe question arises:\nTo what extent are the\nconcepts derived from classical\ncelestial mechanics and applicable to spherical fields valid\nin the immediate\nvicinity of an oblate spheroidal mass ? Of perhaps even greater significance are the questions regarding\nthe validity\nof our concepts with respect\nto the various\ngeophysical\nforces other than gravity that influence the orbit of an Earth satellite.\nSo far\nas practical\nmeasuring\ntechniques\nare concerned (assuming\nproper professional\nuse of\nthe equipment),\nthere is little left to be desired with respect to data density and precision (internal\naccuracy)\nof data obtained by\nmeans of Doppler\nshift in radio frequency.\nHowever,\neven when care in the necessary\ntime and spatial distribution\nof the measurements is exercised,\nthere remains\nsufficient\nreason to suspect that even today occasional\nsystematic\nerrors creep in, not so much as the\nresult of lack of reliability\nin the equipment\nbut as of uncertainties\nin the corrections\nthat\nare necessary\nto transform\nthe velocity\nof\nlight in vacuo into the wave propagation\nvelocity existing at the time of observation.\nThe frequencies\nin use at the present are particularly\naffected by periodic\nchanges in the\nionosphere.\nThe possibility\nof calibrating\nfrequencymeasuring\nequipment\nby comparison\nwith\ndata from laser-type\nDME, by way of position and time determination,\noffers little hope\nin a long-term\nprogram,\nif only for sighting\nreasons.\nParticularly\nineffective\nin this connection have been the unsuccessful\nattempts\nto initiate an efficient and sufficiently\nextensive calibration\nprogram\nin which all the\nmeasuring\nmethods to be used are systematically examined under typical observation\ncon-\n\nS/_TELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nditions by simultaneous\norbit observations\nfrom previously\nand precisely\nsurveyed\nobservation\nsites. The method followed at present of judging\nthe metric\naccuracy\nof the\nvarious\nprocedures\nfrom the internal\naccuracy of, at times, very arbitrarily\nselected\nseries of observations,\nor at any rate of deriving absolute accuracy from the differences\nbetween\nend results\nof measuring\nsystems\nquite different in the techniques\nused in measuring and adjusting,\nis unsatisfactory\nfor the\nmetrological\nengineer\nin general\nand the\ngeodesist in particular\n(see ch. 1).\nIn addition\nto the previously\nmentioned\nGEOS satellites,\nserving\nprimarily\nthe purposes of dynamic satellite geodesy, a balloontype satellite\n(PAGEOS)\nwas used exclusively for the purpose\nof geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation\nwithin\nthe framework\nof the\nNGSP\n(see ch. 5). The balloon, with a 30meter diameter,\nis similar\nin material\nand\nconstruction\nto the balloon satellite ECHO-1\nand has a casing 0.013 mm thick that specutarly reflects\nsunlight,\nunlike the ECHO-2\nsatellite,\nwhose somewhat\nthicker casing has\na more diffusely reflecting\nsurface. PAGEOS\n(passive\ngeodetic satellite)\nwas launched\n23\nJune 1966, in a nearly circular,\nnearly polar\norbit at a height of about 4200 km above the\nearth.\nWith the launching\nwas established\nan elevated\ntarget\nsuitable\nfor worldwide\nsatellite triangulation.\nThe U. S. Coast and\nGeodetic\nSurvey\n(later the National\nOcean\nSurvey within the National\nOceanic and Atmospheric\nAdministration),\ntogether\nwith\nNational Aeronautics\nand Space Administration and the Department\nof Defense, set up a\nworldwide\nnetwork\nof tracking\nstations\nto\ntake advantage\nof PAGEOS.\nThe coordinates\nof the stations\nare given in table 1.28; the\nnetwork\nis shown graphically\nin figure 1.2 of\nchapter 1.\nThe compromise\nin the distribution\nof the\nstations\nnecessitated\nby logistic and political\nconsiderations\nrepresents\na good approximation to an optimal solution.\nThe open mesh\nin the South Pacific Ocean is due to a lack of\nany kind of island, whereas\nthe open space\nover central\nAsia obviously\nresults\nfrom a\npolitical situation.\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nThe triangulation\nmethod based on photogrammetric\nprinciples\nwill be described in detail in section 7.4.\n7.2\n\nINSTRUMENTATION\n\n7.2.1\n7.2.1.1\n\nPhotogrammetric\n\nCamera\n\nGeneral\n\nThe techniques\nused in the measurement\nof\nrocket\ntrajectories,\nparticularly\nbecause\nof\ntheir\nhigh accuracy\nrequirements,\nhad an\nearly influence on the development\nof photogrammetric\ndata acquisition\nand evaluation\ninstrumentation.\nCharacteristic\nin this field\nof application\nis the necessity\nto combine a\ngreat number of observations\nin a single photogram\nin which each individual observation\nis generally\nregistered\nvery\naccurately\nagainst\na time or frequency\nstandard.\nConsequently, there is a requirement\non the photogrammetric\ninstrumentation\nfor great stability over extended\nperiods of observation.\nThis requirement\nled to the development\nof\nthe so-called ballistic camera,\nwhich, on the\nwhole, is based on the phototheodolite\nof terrestrial\nphotogrammetry.\nIn order to adapt\nthe instruments\nto the unorthodox\nrequirements of the geometry\nencountered\nin tracking rocket trajectories\nand at the same time\nincrease\nthe accuracy\nof the direction\ndetermination,\ncameras\nwere developed that could\nbe arbitrarily\noriented\nand that had objectives with long focal lengths.\nA corresponding decrease in viewing angle is inevitable because of practical\nlimitations\non the size of\nthe plate. Exact elements of exterior\norientation are obtained\nwith the use of elements\nfrom the classical\ngeodetic\nangle-measuring\ninstruments,\nsuch as precision\nspindles, circles, and hypersensitive\nlevels.\nThe development\nof instrumentation\nreflecting these concepts\nreached a high point\nin the 1940\'s with the Askania\nphototheodolite (Lacman,\n1950). This camera had a 370mm focal length, f/5.5, 13- by 18-cm plate\nformat,\nand a synchronous\ndrive for the rotary shutters,\nproducing\n1.5, 3, 6, and 12 exposures\nper second with a synchronization\n\nSURVEY\n\n531\n\naccuracy\nof 10 .3 sec. In addition,\na louver\nshutter was available to block out certain exposures in the sequence or to generate\ntimerelated star trails.\nThe horizontal\nand vertical circles could be set to within 3%\nThese instruments\nwere used in Peenemtinde for measuring\nthe V-2 trajectory\nup\nto the point of engine cutoff. On the resumption of similar projects\nin the United States\nafter 1945, interest\nin photogrammetric\nprecision metrology\nfaded\nbecause\nof, as it\nturned out, too-optimistic\nexpectations\nfrom\nelectronic\napproacheszto\nthe problem of trajectory\nmeasurements.\nWhen\nit became\napparent\nthat neither\nthese electronic\napproaches nor the capability\nof the cinetheodolite could do justice to the developing\nrocket\ntechnique,\nthe author\nhad the opportunity,\nin connection\nwith his assigned duties at the\nBallistic Research\nLaboratories\nof the Aberdeen Proving\nGround, Maryland,\nto initiate\nplans for improved\nprecision\ntheodolites,\nresulting, with the active and sympathetic\ncooperation of *_ Swiss _\n_xr;,,_ _,_\n.....\nin today\'s well-known\nBC-4 phototheodolite\nsystem.\nAt the time, the experience\ngained\nin the various fields of experimentation\ncreated a demand for the development\nof a series\nof cameras\nwith different\nangles of view, to\nbe used interchangeably\nto a great extent on\nthe same mount. In addition to the necessary\nvariation\nin picture\nsequence\nover a wide\nrange, the requirement\nfor maximum\naccuracy in exposure\nsynchronization\nwas considered of utmost importance.\nThe development of the complete\nsystem stretched\nout\nover a period of 10 years, and the general\nconcept is described\nin Schmid (1962).\nThe\ntechnical details of the BC-4 phototheodolite\nare explained\nin the literature\nof WildHeerbrugg.\nIn the early 1960\'s the idea of applying the\nphotogrammetric\ntechnique\nfor\ngeometric\nsatellite triangulation\nto the establishment\nof\na continental\nnet began to be seriously\nconsidered\n(U.S.\nDepartment\nof Commerce,\n1965). The technical\nrequirements\nfor such\na project differ from those of conventional\ntrajectory\nmensuration\nin that first, the exterior orientation\nof the camera is not deter-\n\nr\n\n532\n\nNATIONAL GEODETIC SATELLITE PROGRAM\n\nmined with graduated circles, but is determined from the photogram itself on the basis\nof the photographed control points (star\nimages), and second, the unusually high accuracy demands require that the parameters\nneeded for the reconstruction of the photogrammetric bundle (the generalized model of\ninterior orientation) be recomputed for each\nindividual plate. This makes i t necessary to\neffect a compromise between focal length (increase of intrinsic accuracy) and a field of\nview large enough to enable the observer to\nrecord a sufficient number of available catalogued stars in any portion of the sky. Absolute synchronization between the widely\nseparated stations is complicated, actually\nimpossible in theory, in view of the unknown\nlight travel time a t the instant of observation.\nHence, in satellite triangulation it is merely\nnecessary t o record the instants of satellite\nobservation very accurately (to a t least IO-\'\nsee) against the frequency standard at each\nstation. However, the station clocks (frequency standards) must be calibrated with\nrespect to a basically arbitrary but uniquely\ndefined time sequence. Synchronization of the\nclocks to within about\nsee is attained by\nperiodic comparison a t all stations with a\ntraveling calibration clock, which in turn is\ncompared at regular intervals with an atomic\nstandard (e.g., that of the U S . Naval Observatory). Stations with limited accessibility\nare additionally equipped with a cesium\nstandard. The transmission, via satellites, of\ntime signals for clock comparisons has proved\nquite satisfactory, with accuracies of from\n* 2 to & 10 psec, depending on the electronic\nequipment available a t the receiving station.\nThese procedures assure the elimination of\nthe error source due to uncertainties in the\npropagation of light and reduce all other timing errors below the overall error level of the\nsystem. All other residual errors can therefore be neglected in the adjustment.\nE. A. Taylor describes the BC-4 installation as modified to the specifications of the\nU.S. Coast and Geodetic Survey from the\noriginal missile trajectory instrumentation.\nThe present BC-4 phototheodolite differs\nfrom the installation described by E. A. Tay-\n\nlor mainly in the optic now in use, a special\nobjective designed by Dr. Bertele and constructed by the Wild-Heerbrugg Co., which,\ntaking all theoretical and practical considerations into account, represents a n optimal solution for satellite triangulation. The Cosmotar objective has a focal length of 450 mm\nwith a relative opening of 1:3.4, its chief advantage lying in the fact that it has minimal\nchange of radial distortion within the visible\nspectrum. This practically eliminates differences in radial distortion for the centroids of\nimages of stars of various colors and of the\nsun\'s image reflected from the satellite. Although it is\n\n534\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\npossible, even necessary\n(cf. sec. 7.3.1), to\nrecord stars during the period of the satellite\npassage, only low-magnitude\nstars will register is\nof finite length even for relatively\nshort intervals of exposure.\n\n7.2.1.3\n\nElectronic\n\nControl Instrumentation\n\nThe electronic control performs\nthe following principal functions\n:\n(1) Drives the 500-Hz synchronous\nmotor\nof the rotary disk shutter\nfrom the built-in\nstation frequency\nstandard.\n(2) Controls\nthe station\nclock with the\nsame frequency\nstandard.\n(3) Synchronizes\nthe signal from the magnetic pickup of the rotating\ndisk shutter with\nthe station clock.\n(4) Controls both shutter\nsystems.\n(5) Illuminates\nthe fiducial marks and the\ndisplay of auxiliary\ndata for later identification evaluation.\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nsimilar electronic\nprogram\nin the synchronization system.\nThis results\nin a display on\nthe oscilloscope\nof a pulse sequence for the\ntime code generator\ncorresponding\nto the selected program\n(for example, two exposures\nper second).\nSimultaneously,\nthe pulses from\nthe magnetic\npickup, indicating\nthe mid-open\nposition of the shutters,\nare fed to the oscillograph.\nBy a phase comparison\nthe two, in\ntime initially\ndifferent,\nsignals\nare brought\ninto coincidence,\neffecting\nsynchronization\nbetween\nstation clock and exposure.\nOwing\nto practical\nlimitations\nin the mechanical\nprecision of the drive, there are slight irregularities in the shutter\nrates, causing\nthe signal\nreturning\nfrom the cameras\nto vary irregularly\nin time with the comparison\nsignal\noriginating\nin the station clock. These deviations are of the order of from \xc3\xb720 to \xc2\xb140\n_sec. The actual synchronization\nprocess is,\ntherefore,\nto give the signal from the station\nclock an adjustable\nbandwidth\nto each side\nof, for example,\n100 t_sec. If the signal from\nthe BC-4 falls within this gate, it is registered\nas synchronous\non the oscillograph\ntape ; otherwise,\nit is not. The plates\nare either 6 or !0 mm thick, and those of best\n\nSURVEY\n\n537\n\nquality have a flatness of 3 _m. A good compromise between sensitivity\nand grain size is\nfound in the Eastman\nKodak emulsion 103-F.\nAfter accurately\ncontrolled\ndeveloping\nof the\nplate, particular\ncare must be exercised in the\ndrying\nprocess:\nthe plate must be turned\ncontinuously.\nThe essential\ninformation\ncontent of an individual\nphotogram\nconsists of\nthe point-shaped\nstar images and the satellite\ntrail. For star registration\na sequence of five\nsuccessive individual images is necessary,\nfor\nstatistical\nreasons. In order to obtain uniform\nstar images independent\nof star magnitude,\nit is necessary\nto expose several\nsuch sequences with various shutter\nspeeds. The selection of optimal exposure\ntime is, in addition, dependent\non the range in declination\nof the stars.\nStar photography,\nusing the\ncapping shutter, is executed before as well as\nafter the satellite\npass. During\nthe satellite\npass, additional\nimages of the brighter\nstars\nin the field of view are generated\nby appropriate programing\nof the rotating\ndisk shutters. These stcl!ar images are of particular\nimportance\nfor the exterior orientation,\nsince\nthey are recorded\nsimultaneously\nwith the\nsatellite trail.\nWith suitable choice of exposure interval it is possible to obtain a presentation of both stars and the satellite in a series of similar point-shaped\nimages.\nIn measuring\nthe negative itself, one is presented with the problem of centering\na black\nmeasuring\nmark within a dark point-shaped\nimage.\n(To date--1973--ring-shaped\nmeasuring marks with a diameter\nof 20 to 30 _m\nare not available.)\nA series of experiments\nhas shown that the plate measurement\nprocess is faster and more reliable if a diapositive\nis first produced, so that the black measuring\nmark can be set within a white round image.\nThe negatives\nare copied in almost monochromatic\nblue light under vacuum onto a 6mm-thick glass plate covered with an exceedingly fine-grained\nemulsion.\nStatistical\ntests\nhave shown that the copying process introduces no marked\ndeterioration\nin accuracy.\nFigure 7.12 shows the star and satellite\nimages schematically.\nThe sequences\nA, B, C,\nand D, E, F represent\nfive star images, each\ntaken with the capping\nshutter\nand various\n\n538\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nurement\nof photograms\nis one of the most essential\nphases\nof analytical\nphotogrammetry\nand as such has basically\narbitrary\norigin.\nThe\ncomparator\nused\ndoes not have\nto operate\nnecessarily\non this principle,\nbut can, for example,\nmeasure\npolar\ncoordinates\ninstead\n(D, C. Brown,\nunpublished).\nThese\nmust,\nhowever,\nbe transformed\nto the x, y coordinates needed\nlater in the adjustment,\nand the\ncorresponding\nweight\nmatrix,\ncorrelated\nin\nthis case, must be computed.\nSince the meas-\n\nliterature,\nand since,\nfurthermore,\nthe specific measuring\nmethod\nused depends\nnot only\non the type\nof comparator\nused but also on\nthe organizational\nand environmental\nconditions,\nonly those\nphases\nthat\nare typical\nfor\nthe problem\nin question\nbut do not necessarily\nhave applicability\nfor other more or less conventional\nworking\nprocedures\nwill be discussed.\nIf a high degree\nof accuracy\nin the end results\nof geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation\nis\nto be achieved,\nit is necessary\nto bear in mind\nfrom\nthe outset\nthe fact that a large\nnumber\nof points\n(600 to 750 star images\nand up to\n600 satellite\nimages)\nmust\nbe measured\non\neach photogram,\nand consequently\nthat\n5 to\n8 hours\nare required\nfor the measurement.\nSpecial\ncare must\ntherefore\nbe exercised\nin\nthe selection\nof the type of comparator,\nthe\nenvironmental\nconditions,\nand the arrangement\nof working\nprocedures,\nso that\nsystematic\nerror\ninfluences\ncan be held to a minimum or can be corrected\ncomputationally.\nA\ndescription\nof the current\nprocedures\nat the\nU. S. National\nOcean Survey\n(NOS)\nfollows.\nThe measurements\nare made on comparators\nequipped\nwith\nindependent\nx and\ny\nscrews\nwith a working\nlength\nof about\n225\nmm each.\nThe instruments,\nmanufactured\nby\nthe firm of David\nMann,\nLincoln,\nMassachusetts,\nare equipped\nwith\na direct\nbinocular\nmicroscope,\nmagnification\nadjustable\nin steps\nup to 40\xc3\x97,\nand a circular\nmeasuring\nmark\n(dot)\nwith a diameter\nof about\n30 _m.\nThe\ncomparators\nare operated\nin a controlled\nenvironment\n(temperature\n22 \xc2\xb0 C_+ 0.5 \xc2\xb0, humidity 50%_+5%)\nand\ntested\nabout\nevery\n2\nmonths\nfor linear\nand periodic\nscale\nerrors\nin the x and y screws\nas well as for orthogonality\nof the\nmotions.\nCalibrated\ngrid\nplates\nin each of four positions\nare measured\nfor this purpose\nby each of three\nobservers.\nThe\nmeasured\ncoordinates\nto the\nnearest\nmicron\nare\nregistered\nelectronically\non a\ntypewriter,\npunched\ntape,\nor card.\nThe initial operations\nrevealed\nthe fact that the operator\'s\nbody heat generated\nan unacceptably\n\nNATIONAL GEODETIC SURVEY\n\nFIGURE\n7.13.-Star\n\nplate.\n\n539\n\n540\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nlarge systematic\nerror in the measurements,\nbecause the optics of the comparator\nwas not\nconstructed\nin accordance\nwith the Abbe\nprinciple.\nIt was necessary,\ntherefore,\nto\nmodify the construction\nto project the measuring mark into the plane of measurement.\nExtensive\ntests have shown that the comparators\nnow are stable for operating\nperiods of from 2 to 3 hours. However, it is still\nnecessary to subdivide the measurement\nof a\nplate into several such periods.\nThe first step is to drill eight circular\nholes\ninto the emulsion, approximately\n40 to 50 _m\nin diameter.\nThese are located at the four\ncorners,\nthe extremities\nof the legs of the\nconventional\nfiducial marks pointing toward\nthe center, and at the approximate\ncenter of\neach edge. These drill holes are measured\nat\nthe beginning and end of each measuring\nsession, and the differences\nare used to cheek the\nstability\nof the instrument\nduring that period. Before continuing\nwith the descriptionof the measuring\nprocedure,\nsome essentials\non the preparation\nfor plate measurement\nmust be mentioned.\nThe readings on the circles in the field give\nan orientation\nof the camera in azimuth and\nelevation\nto within\n10 to 20\'.\' With the\ntime of observation\nand approximate\nstation\ncoordinates,\na range in right ascension\nand\ndeclination\non the celestial\nsphere\ncan be\ncomputed.\nThe computer\nsearches\nthe star\ncatalog tape for all stars in this portion\nof\nthe sky, and their coordinates\ntogether\nwith\nthe nominal camera\nconstants\nand the approximated\norientation\ndata are used to compute plate coordinates\nfor these stars.\nThese\npoints are projected\non a cathode-ray\ntube associated with the electronic computer and are\nphotographed\nto the scale of the photogram\nto produce a star chart.\nThe stars are subdivided into three groups of magnitudes\nand\nlabeled accordingly.\nAnother\nsymbol designates a group of at least eight stars as bright\nas possible and located in a circular ring 3 em\nwide near the edges of the plate.\nSince the\nregistration\non the original\nphotogram\nor\ndiapositive\nvaries\naccording\nto magnitude,\nit is easy to bring the photogram\nand the\n"computed\nstar chart" into coincidence\non a\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nlight table. At the same time, a grid template\nis superimposed,\ndividing\nthe plate format\ninto 100 equal squares.\nThe photogram\nis\nnow examined under the binocular\nmagnification of the comparator.\nIn each of the squares\na star of the series, before and after the satellite pass, that coincides with an image on the\nstar chart is selected and marked.\nIn addition, all stars recorded\nduring\nthe satellite\npass (cf. fig. 7.12) and the specially selected\nbright stars near the edge of the plate are\nmarked with an identification\nsymbol.\nAfter this preparation,\nthe plate is placed\nin the comparator.\nIn order to eliminate\nas\nfar as possible the influence of unknown\nsystematic errors, a subgroup\nof stars and satellite images covering\nthe whole extent of the\nplate is measured\nat each of the two or three\nsessions required.\nAt the completion\nof the\nmeasurements\nall premarked\nstars and satellite images will have been measured.\nIn connection\nwith the satellite\ntrail it should be\nadded that it crosses the plate within at most\na few millimeters\nof the center and its images are measured\nto a maximum\ndistance of\n6 cm from the center, in order to avoid edge\neffects in the emulsion.\nTo combine in a consistent system the reading obtained from the\ntwo or three necessary\ncomparator\nsittings,\nthe individual\nsets are translated,\nrotated,\nand stretched\nwith two scale corrections\nin\nadjustment,\nin accordance\nwith the coordinates of the relevant\ndrill holes to best fit\nthe configuration\nof drill holes, showing the\nsmallest\nmean error.\nThe residuals\nof the\nreference\npoints after these transformations\nare typically\n0.3 _m. The entire measuring\nprocess is then repeated\nwith the plate turned\nthrough approximately\n180 degrees. Both results are then meaned by fitting the latter result to the first, again by means of an adjustment\n(determination\nof two components\nof\ntranslation,\na rotation,\nand two scale factors). From the residual\ndifferences\nbetween\ncorresponding\ndouble measurements\nin this\nadjustment,\na characteristic\nmean error\nof\n1.6 _m results\nas a measure\nof precision\nof\nthe measured\ncoordinates.\nIn addition, the plate coordinates\nof all premarked star and satellite images are referred\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nto the plate center as determined\nby the fiducial marks.\nThe coordinates\nof the abovementioned\nbright stars near the edges of the\nplate that are easily identifiable\nin the catalog are now used to compute an approximate\norientation.\nWith this result,\nright ascensions and declinations\nare computed from the\nimage coordinates\nof all measured\nstars\n(cf.\nsec. 7.4.6). The same program\ncompares these\nvalues with the tape containing\nthe star catalog, identifies\nthe stars, and updates them to\nthe observation\nepoch and true equinox\n(cf.\nsec. 7.4.3, eqs. 7.1-7.15).\nThe step in the overall adjustment\nprocedure of satellite\ntriangulation\nunder discussion\nrepresents\na mutually\ninteracting\ncombination\nof human effort and electronic\ncomputing.\nThe contributions\nfrom the human element,\nsuch as the execution\nof the\nmeasurements\nand evaluation\nof the statistical intermediate\nresults, are the critical\noperations ; the computing\nsystem prepares\nstar\ncharts, presents\ncatalogued\ndata, and makes\nthe necessary\ncomputations.\nAfter completion\nof this operation,\nmeasured coordinates\nfor all selected\nstars and\nsatellite\nimages are available,\nas well as the\nstar coordinates\nreduced\nup to a certain\npoint. These data are now further\nreduced in\na numerical\nadjustment\nto be discussed\nin\nsection 7.4.6.\n7.4\n7.4.1\n\nTHEORY\nIntroductory Considerations\n\nIn the classical treatment\nof geometric\ngeodesy, i.e., the part of geodesy that concerns\nitself with the derivation\nof rigorous geometric results, difficulties arise from the fact that\nthe measured\nquantities\ncannot be rigorously\nrelated\nto the geometric\nmodel that is to be\nestablished.\nPhysical\ninfluences\nare responsible for this dilemma. The so-called measurements of horizontal\nand vertical\nangles are\nvitiated to an unknown\nextent by systematic\ninfluences\nsuch as anomalies\nin the gravity\nfield and refraction.\nThe reduction\nof baseline measurements\nis in principle\nsimilarly\naffected.\n\nSURVEY\n\n541\n\nIn addition,\nthe classical\nmethod of triangulation is forced to adopt a number of complex postulates\nwhose geometric\ncontent\nis\nbased on certain hypotheses.\nTypical examples are the present-day\ncorrection\nmethods\ngenerally known as "isostatic\nreduction\nprocedures."\nThe physical principle\nunderlying\nthese procedures\nis the assumption\nof homogeneity and hydrostatic\nequilibrium\nof the\nmasses within the Earth\'s\ncrust. The resulting corrections\nto all geodetic\nobservations\nwill prej udice the end result in favor of Clairaut\'s theory. Aside from the physical assumptions, an unavoidable\ncharacteristic\nof classical geodetic\ntriangulation\nconsists\nof the\npractical\nlimitation\nof sight length between\npoints on or near the surface\nof the Earth.\nNot only are such geodetic triangulations\nincapable of making\nintercontinental\nconnections, but the first-order\nnets must be pieced\ntogether\nwith an excessive\nnumber\nof individual arcs. The disadvantage\nof this method\narises not so much from the relatively\nlarge\nnumber of stations involved as from the fact\nthat accuracy is impaired,\nespecially in extensive nets, by error propagation.\nAs a consequence,\ngeodetic theory has developed complex methods\nof adjustment\ndesigned to eliminate\nthe contradictions\nin the\ndata by iteration,\npermitting\nthe results\nof\npartly geometric\nand partly geophysical\nadjustment\noperations\nto interact\nuntil all results become internally\nconsistent.\nAlthough\nthey are attractive\nfrom a theoretical\nstandpoint, such methods\nhave practical\nlimitations. For this reason a purely geometrically\ndefined, three-dimensional\nworldwide\ngeodetic reference\nsystem is desired\nin order to\ntranscend\nthe shortcomings\nof the classical\ngeodetic\ntriangulation\nmethod.\nMoreover,\nsuch a worldwide\ngeometric\nsolution is superior to a mere connection\nof the various\ngeodetic datums, which has at times been called\nthe purpose of satellite geodesy.\nThe significance\nof a three-dimensional\ntriangulation\nmethod, emphasized\nrepeatedly\nin\nthe recent history\nof geodesy, becomes especially apparent\nin connection\nwith the field of\nsatellite geodesy, which because of its geometric and geophysical\naspects demands a three-\n\n542\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\ndimensional\nsolution.\nPerhaps\nthe greatest\nsignificance\nof geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation, however, lies in the fact that with this\nmethod there exists, for the first time in the\nhistory\nof geodesy, the possibility\nfor the\ncreation\nof a worldwide\nthree-dimensional\nreference system that is supported\nby a minimum of a priori hypotheses,\nin particular\nwithout reference\nto either the magnitude\nor\nthe direction of the force of gravity.\nEstablishing\ngeometric\ncorrespondence\namong a number\nof selected nonintervisible\npoints of the physical\nsurface\nof the Earth\ncan be accomplished\nwith spatial triangulation by means of auxiliary\ntargets\nelevated\nsufficiently above the Earth\'s\nsurface.\nThe generation\nof light signals visible over\nlarge distances is possible by means of artificial satellites.\nBecause of the high velocity\nof such targets,\nobservation\nof directions\nto\nthem can at present be made only with photogrammetric\nprecision cameras.\nOwing to the\nphysical and chemical\nproperties\nof the photogrammetric\nmeasurement\ncomponents,\nthe\nabsolute accuracy\nas well as the reproducibility of the observation\nconditions\nin this\nmethod is limited.\nTo obtain observational\nresults with maximum\nabsolute accuracy, the\nadjustment\nof the photogrammetric\nmeasurements must be based on a method of interpolation.\nA suitable reference\nsystem into which an\nelevated target\ncan be intercalated\nis obviously the right ascension-declination\nsystem\nof metric astronomy.\nThis system is all the\nmore attractive\nfrom the geodetic\npoint of\nview because one of its axes is parallel to the\nEarth\'s\naxis of rotation.\nA large number of\nfixed stars are available\nwhose coordinates\nare tabulated\nin catalogs.\nThese\ncontrol\npoints being practically\nat an infinite\ndistance, it follows that their direction\ncoordihates are insensitive\nto a parallel\ndisplacement of the observer\nand hence cannot be\nused for scale determination.\nIt is therefore\nnecessary to determine\nthe scale of the satellife triangulation\nindependently:\nfor example, by measuring\nthe distance\nbetween two\nadjacent\nstations.\nAs will be shown later\n(sec. 7.4.3), it is necessary\nto carry out such\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nscale determinations\nin several\nthe worldwide\ntriangulation\nnet.\n7.4.2\n\nportions\n\nof\n\nGeometric Foundations\n\nWe turn our attention\nnow to a threedimensional\nmethod of triangulation\nthat is\nbased on direction measurement\nand designed\nto determine\nthe coordinates\nof nonintervisible triangulation\nstations.\nThe relevant geometric solution is not new.\nIn fact, there is little room for originality\nin\nthe field of the application\nof photogrammerry to ballistic and related problems.\nThe\nuse of star photography\nfor the calibration\nof\nphotogrammetric\ncameras\nis\na\nproven\nmethod, especially with astronomers.\nThe use\nof star images\nto orient\nphotogrammetric\ncameras\nand the corresponding\ntriangulation\nof additionally\nphotographed\ntarget\npoints\nwas used successfully\nin the 1930\'s by Hopmann and Lohmann\n(1943) in the tracking\nof missiles before the method was applied in\nthe development\nof the V-2 rocket at Pennemfinde, Germany,\nand subsequently\nin various other countries.\nThere\nare several\nways\nto present\nthe\ngeometric\nprinciples\nof this triangulation\nmethod.\nV_is_l_\'s\nproposal\ncontains\na lucid\ngeometric\nexplanation.\nTwo rays\nissuing\nfrom the end-points\nof a given baseline and\ndirected\nat a common point define a plane in\nspace whose orientation\ncan be determined\nfrom the direction\ncosines of the rays. When\ntwo such planes have been fixed, the direction\nin space of the baseline\ncan be computed\nas\nthe intersection\nof the two planes.\nThe principle involved\nis shown in figures 7.14 and\n7.15.\nAfter two directions\nAB and AC issuing\nfrom station A have been determined\nin this\nfashion, the shape and spatial orientation\nof\nthe station triangle\nABC is fixed by intersecting AB and AC with a plane whose orientation is known from observing\nthe satellite\nposition S_ from B and C. Thus, five planes\nare necessary\nand sufficient to fix the shape\nand orientation\nof a station triangle.\nEach\nof these planes contains two stations and one\npoint of the satellite\norbit; therefore,\nthere\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\n543\n\nSURVEY\n\n$3\n\ns\n/\n\n,/[////\n-_/\ni/ i/\n\n/ _/i/\n\n\'$\n\n/\n/\n\n\'\n\n/\n\nFIGURE\n\nS iI\n\nx.\n\ni\n\nf"-,"/\'!/ I/" t Il\nl\n/ / i\n"//i/_i/"\nFIGURE\n\n7.14\n\nare five positions\nof the orbit, together\nwith\nthe positions of the three stations A, B, C, or\n(5+3) \xc3\x97 3--24 unknowns\nto be determined.\nSince each pair of simultaneous\nobservations of a satellite\nposition--or,\nin other\nwords, the determination\nin space of two intersecting\nlines--gives\nrise to four equations,\nthere are, in all, 5 \xc3\x97 4 = 20 equations available.\nHence,\n24-20=4\nadditional\nindependently\ndetermined\ngeometric\nquantities\nare required\nfor a complete\nsolution of the triangle.\nThe\nmost obvious of the many theoretically\navailable choices are the three coordinates\nof one\nof the stations,\nwhich, in principle,\ncan be\nassumed arbitrarily,\nfor example, as the origin of the coordinate\nsystem.\nIt is equally\nlogical to choose as the fourth\nassumption\nthe length of one of the sides of the triangle,\nwhich fixes the scale for the whole triangulation. For purposes\nof explaining\nthe principle of satellite triangulation\nit is sufficient to\nintroduce\nthis side-length\nas the unit of\nlength.\nIt is interesting\nto note that three of the\nfive necessary\nplanes can be determined\nwith\na single pass of the satellite,\nif the satellite\nsubpoint\nlies near the middle of the triangle\n\n7.15\n\nof stations\n(see fig. 7.16).\nFor this case, parameters\nmust be introduced.\nFrom the viewpoint\nof analytical\nphotogrammetry,\nthe geometric\nprinciple\nof\nsatellite\ntriangulation\ncan be explained\nby\nidentifying\nthe unknown positions of the triangulation\nstations and the unknown\norientations\nof the observing\ncameras\nwith the\ncorresponding\nconditions\nin classical\naerial\nphotogrammetry.\nThe unknown\norbital positions of the satellite\ncorrespond\nto the relative control points, with the restriction\nthat\nthey cannot furnish scale, since they lie at an\ninfinite distance.\nThe geometric\nconcept of photogrammetric\nsatellite triangulation\nmust, however,\nbe interpreted\nin the light of the fact that at each\nstation the stars (absolute control points) are\nused for the determination\nof the elements of\nthe interior\norientation\nnecessary\nfor the reconstruction\nof the In\naddition to the theoretically\ndesirable\nthreedimensional\ncharacter\nof the triangulation\nmethod,\nit will be a question\nof economic\nfeasibility\nwhether the development\nof such a\ntechnique\nwill, in part, replace classical firstorder triangulation\nin certain\nareas of the\nEarth.\nQuite independently\nof the measurements\nof the individual\nspatial\ntriangulation\nfigures, the basic geometrical\nconcept underlying the method of satellite\ntriangulation\nrequires, at least in principle,\nsimultaneously\nexecuted\nobservations\nof directions\nto "the\ntarget (in our case the satellite)\nfrom at least\ntwo stations.\nClearly,\nthe requirement\nto\nmeasure directions\nrather than merely angles\nimplies the necessity\nof orienting\nthe observed\nbundle\nin each case relative\nto a\nuniquely defined system of reference.\nThis is\notherwise\nself-evident\nin view of the fact\nthat our end result\nis to represent\na consistent spatial reference\nsystem.\nA spatial\ncoordinate\nsystem\nto which a\ndirection\nto a target\nsufficiently\nelevated\nabove the horizon call be --_ ...... _ "- *_^ _\'_*\nascension-declination\nsystem.\nThis reference\nsystem,\nsurrounding\nthe whole\nEarth,\nis\nqualitatively\nas well as quantitatively\nsuitable, a great number\nof precisely\nmeasured\nreference\npoints being readily available.\nOf\nespecial significance\nto the photogrammetric\nmensuration\nprinciple\nis the abundance\nof\nsuch absolute\ncontrol\npoints,\nsince because\nof the physical\nand chemical\nnature\nof its\nnumerous\ncomponents\nand procedures\nthe\nphotogrammetric\nmethod can satisfy the requirements\nfor highest\naccuracy\nonly if the\ncorresponding\nobservational\nand adjustment\nprocedures\nare executed\nin a close-interval\ninterpolation\nprocess.\nWe emphasize\nhere, therefore,\nthat the\nclaim of satellite\ntriangulation,\nthat it produces results without the aid of physical hypotheses\nand practically\nfree of systematic\nerrors, derives chiefly from the fact that photogrammetric\ndirection\ndetermination\nin satellite triangulation\nnot only operates with the\naid of geometric\ninterpolation\nwithin the reconstructed\nphotogrammetric\nbundle but rep-\n\n545\n\nSURVEY\n\nresents equally an interpolation\ninto the physical process of astronomic\nrefraction\n(see\nsec. 7.4.5). This also means that the absolute\naccuracy\nof photogrammetric\nsatellite\ntriangulation\ndepends primarily\non the quality\nof the right\nascension-declination\nsystem,\nparticularly\non its freedom from systematic\nerrors.\nOn account of the importance\nof the\nastronomic\nreference\nsystem to satellite\ntriangulation,\nsome relevant\nremarks\nwill be\nmade in the next section, specifically\nas they\napply to the data processing\nin satellite\ntriangulation.\n7.4.3\n\nAstronomical Reference System 1,,\n\nIn satellite\ntriangulation,\nphotographing\nthe fixed stars serves to reconstruct\nand to\nuniquely orient in space the photogrammetric\nbundle.\nThe problem\nof reconstructing\nthe\nbundle is fundamentally\nidentical\nwith the\nproblem\nof calibrating\na photogrammetric\ncamera.\nThe geometric\ninterpretation\nof the\nrelevant\nparameters\nis independent\nof the\norientation\nof the camera. It would therefore\nsuffice to have given the relative\ngeometric\narrangement\nof the images of the stars on a\nparticular\nplate in an arbitrary\ncoordinate\n1 For the proper\ninterpretation\nof the\ntions\nin this\nsection\nit is recommended\nreader\n\nfirst\n\nstudy\n\nsections\n\n7.4.6\n\nand\n\ncomputathat\nthe\n\n7.4.8.\n\nThe s3_bolism\nused in this chapter\nis common\nin\nphotogrammetric\npractice,\nand the author\ndid not\nwish it changed.\nSome of the symbols\nare used with\ndifferent\nmeanings\noutside\nof photogrammetry\n(in\nparticular\nin geodesy\nand astronomy)\nand even occur\nin this chapter\nwith two or more meanings.\nTherefore, care\nshould\nbe exercised\nin interpreting\nthe\nequations\nin this chapter.\nFor this reason,\nthe following\nambiguities\nare indicated\nby footnotes:\n(1)\nis used in this chapter\nto denote one of the angles\ndefining\nthe orientation\nof a camera\nand to denote\nright\nascension;\nthe latter\nsense is that used in the\nrest of the volume.\n(2) The symbol c is used to denote one or (with subscripts)\nmore of the characteristics of a camera\nand to denote the velocity\nof light;\nonly the latter\nconvention\nis used in the rest of this\nvolume.\nWhere\nused in this chapter\nin the former\nsense and where\nconfusion\nmay occur, the exceptions\nare noted by (1) a dagger\n(t) for a denoting\none of\nthe angles defining\nthe orientation\nof the camera,\nand\n(2) a double dagger\n(:_) for c denoting\na characteristic of *_\'^ camera,\nwA_,_\n\n546\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nsystem.\nHowever,\nthe determination\nof an\nunambiguous\norientation\nfor all the bundles\nof rays serving\nthe triangulation\nis predicated on the fact that all given control points\n(the totality of fixed stars used) are given in\na uniquely defined reference\nsystem,\nwhich\nfurthermore\ncan be uniquely transformed\nto\nan Earth-fixed\ncoordinate\nframe.\nThe right\nascension-declination\nsystem\nof metric\nastronomy,\nas was previously\nmentioned,\nfurnishes the metric basis for geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation.\nThe point of departure\nis the\napparent\nposition coordinates\nof stars tabulated for a given epoch in star catalogs\nsuch\nas Apparent\nPlaces of 1535 Fundamental\nStars,\npublished\nby the\nAstronomisches\nRechen-Institut,\nHeidelberg;\nthe\nGeneral\nCatalogue\n(Boss, 1936), with approximately\n33 000 apparent\nstar positions;\nor in probably the most complete catalog to date, issued\nby the Smithsonian\nAstrophysical\nLaboratory, in which over 250 000 stars with their\napparent\nplaces are tabulated\n(see chs. 1\nand 9).\nThe choice of stars selected for the purpose\nof satellite triangulation\ndepends\nprimarily\non the accuracy\nof their coordinates.\nStars\nwith large proper motions should be avoided\nand double stars should not be used at all.\nTo counteract\nthe influence\nof spectral\ndifferences\nof the stars, a special lens was\nused (see sec. 7.2). Finally, the selection was\nlimited by the magnitude\nregistered\nby the\nspecific optical\nsystem\nand emulsion\nused.\nWith the BC-4 system\nand the EastmanKodak emulsion 103-F in use today, stars of\nthe seventh and eighth magnitude\nstill produced good, measurable\nimages over the entire plate.\nUsing very bright\nstars at the\nsame time raised the question concerning\nthe\ninfluence\nof relative\nsystematic\nerrors\nin\nlocating the centroid\nof the image.\nWith the\nfocal lengths in use at present\nfor satellite\ntriangulation\npurposes,\nthe existence\nof a\nmagnitude\neffect, which is not negligible\nin\nastronomical\nmeasurements,\nhas not as yet\nbeen quantitatively\ndemonstrated.\nIn Bossler\n(1966),\nthe distribution\nover\nthe celestial\nsphere\nwith respect\nto right\nascension and declination\nof the Smithsonian\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\ncatalog stars is described from the standpoint\nused in the selection.\nGeometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation\ncan at\nbest, therefore,\nattain\nthe accuracy\nof the\nastronomical\nreference\nsystem\n(see\nsec.\n7.3.1).\nHence, for a critical\nstudy of the\ntheoretical\naccuracy of satellite triangulation,\nthe observation\nand adjustment\nprocedures\nused in metric astronomy\nto establish\nstar\ncatalogs\nare of fundamental\nimportance.\nWithin the frame of this presentation\nit must\nsuffice to refer to the literature\non these\nhighly specialized\nand complex procedures:\nClemence,\n1963; Scott, 1957, 1963; Vasilevskis, 1963; Dieckvoss,\n1963; Woolard\nand\nClemence, 1966.\nTo understand\ngeometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation, it is necessary\nto interpret\ncorrectly\nthe qualitative\n(geometric)\nand quantitative\n(statistical)\ndata listed in the star catalogs\nin order to grasp the reductions\nnecessary\nto\ntransform\nthe timeand space-dependent\ngeometry\nof the individual\nphotogrammetric\nexposures\ninto a homogeneous\ngeometric\nsystem. The problems arising in this connection\nare basically\nthe same as those faced in the\nreduction\nof astronomic\ngeodetic\nfield observations.\nThe star catalogs\nlist for apparent\nplaces\na pair of spherical\ncoordinates\nfor a specified\nepoch, right ascension\n_ and declination\n(see ch. 1). The specification\nthat the cataloged values refer to a given epoch (generally\nthe beginning\nof the tropical\nyear 1950.0 in\nthe newer\ncatalogs)\nmeans\nthat\ntime-dependent corrections\nmust be added to the star\ncoordinates\nbefore they represent\nthe actual\nposition at the instant\nof observation,\nin our\ncase the time of exposure.\nThe reason for these corrections\nis chiefly\nto be found in the dynamics\nof the universe,\nalthough\npurely physically\nbased corrections\nmust also be taken into consideration.\nTheoretical explanations\nare described\nin detail in\nstandard\nworks on geodetic astronomy\n(e.g.,\nKSnig,\n1962;\nClemence,\n1966;\nWayman,\n1966 ; Fricke and Kopff, 1963 ; Morgan, 1952 ;\nGliese, 1963; Kopff et al., 1964).\nNevertheless, it seems useful to outline here, in terms\nof formulas,\nthe sequence of corrections\nused.\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nFor one thing, such a presentation\nwill present a computerized\nmethod designed\nto reduce a large number of star places, including\ncircumpolars,\nthat are needed in geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation.\nFor another,\nit helps\nto clarify\nthe contribution\nof the individual\ncorrections\nto the overall adjustment\nprocedure of satellite\ntriangulation\nand to judge\nthe technical\nand economical\naspects\ninvolved.\nThe following\ncomputations\nare made for\nthe purpose of deriving,\nfrom the star catalog\ndata for a specific epoch, that unit vector\nwhich\ndesignates\nthe apparent\ngeocentric\ndirection\nof a star with reference\nto the true\nequinox at the instant\nT of observation.\nThe\nsolution shown here is based on the method\nnow in use at the U. This gives\ny= R(-_st)\n3\n\nLsV\n\nR (270\xc2\xb0 +4,\'y\'_\n2\n\n[c\xc2\xb0sX\'-sin_\'\n\ni]\n0\n\n[\n\n0 0/y\n\nsin4,\'\n0\n-cos 4" 0\n\nc\xc2\xb0s4,\' 7\nsin _,\']\n(7.30)\n\nor analogously,\n_Ry(_, _, K) =_R-#_,_t) R (270 \xc2\xb0 +4,\')\n3\n2\nR_(_, _, _)\n\'\n(7.31)t\nwhere _R,(a, _,, K)t corresponds\ntem to the\nphotogrammetric\nmatrix.\n\nin the y sysorientation\n\nBasically, the aim of the reductions\nso far\n_"....... _ is to refer\nall the photographically registered\ndirections\nto stars--observed\nfrom different\nstations\nand, in general,\nat\ndifferent\ntimes---to\na consistent\nstationary\ncoordinate\nsystem.\nThe computations\nwould\nproduce a rigorous\ngeometric\nsolution only if\nwe could assume\nthat the direction\nof the\nEarth\'s\naxis of rotation,\ni.e., the y_ direction\nof figure 7.18a labeled instantaneous\naxis of\nrotation,\nremains\ninvariant\nin space.\nWe\nknow, however,\nthat the poles describe more\nor less irregular\nloops in a period of approximately 430 days about a mean position which\nitself possibly\nhas a secular\ndisplacement.\nFrom a geometrical\npoint of view it is immaterial\nwhether\nthis so-called polar motion\nis treated as an additional\nmotion relative to\nthe astronomic\nreference\nsystem\n(a sort of\nadditional\nprecession\nand\nnutation)\nor\nwhether one accepts the direction of the rotation axis as invariant\nand ascribes\nthe phenomenon\nto a displacement\nof the crust.\nHowever, in addition to this purely geometric\nand computable\neffect, the influence of polar\nmotion is coupled with the problem of time\ndetermination\nat the observation\nsite. For\nthis reason the discussion of these corrections\n\nSURVEY\n\n553\n\nwill be combined\nwith the questions\nof time\ndetermination\nin the following section.\n7.4.4\n\nMeaning and Measurement of Time\n\nThe significance\nof time determination\nfor\nthe problem of geometric\nsatellite triangulation is twofold.\nFirst, because of the dynamic\ncharacteristics\nof the universe,\ni.e., because\nof the Earth\'s\nmotion in space, we must determine\nthe instant\nof the photographic\nexposure of the star image within an interval\nbased on astronomic\nobservations.\nIn addition, because\nof the motion of the satellite\nitself, the instants of observation\nof the satellife at all stations observing\nthe pass must be\ncorrelated\nwith respect to an otherwise\narbitrary measuring\nfrequency,\nwhich amounts\nto a relative time determination.\nWith this, one interpolates\npoints\nalong\nthe satellite\ntrack\nwhose images,\nfrom\na\ngeometric\nstandpoint,\nrepresent\nbasically\narbitrary\nbut uniquely\ndefined points on the\norbit.\nIn registering\nthe pass of a satellite\nwhose track\nis marked\nby short-duration\nlight flashes this requirement\nis not necessary. Because of the finite speed of light, part\nof the photons emitted\nin the flash will, in\ngeneral, not arrive at the different\nobservation sites simultaneously,\nbut for that very\nreason they will produce images whose positions on the various\nphotograms\ncorrespond\nto a single point in space, the origin of the\nflash, and thus fulfill automatically\nthe "geometric condition of simultaneity."\nFollowing\nis a discussion\nof those problems\nconcerned\nwith the impact on satellite triangulation\nof\nthe time\nof observation\nneeded\nfor star\nimagery.\nWe emphasize\nagain the fact that\nthe requirement\nfor time correlation\nin star\nobservations\nis, in principle,\nof a purely geometric nature.\nThis conclusion\nfollows from\nthe fact that the spatial position of the Earth\nwith its observation\nstations\nchanges\nwith\ntime, relative\nto the astronomic\nreference\nsystem. The measurement\nof time, therefore,\nserves to refer the spatial orientation\nof the\nEarth at the instant\nof observation\nback to\nan orientation\nassumed as a normal position\nand corresponding\nat a specified epoch.\n\n554\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nFor the motion of the Earth\naround\nthe\nSun it is necessary\nto refer the Julian day\nand fraction of a day, as represented\nby UT\nfor the instant\nof observation,\nto the beginning of the corresponding\ntropical\nyear, the\nlatter representing\na point of time independent of all calendar\nreckoning\nand the same\nfor all meridians\n(cf. Jordan/Eggert,\n1939).\nThe interval\nT so determined\nis needed for\nall the reductions\ndescribed\nin section 7.4.3.\nThe interval\nderived from the time of day\nserves\nas the basis for determining\nlocal\nsidereal\ntime in accordance\nwith the steps\ngiven in the previous\nsection.\nThere\nnow\narises in satellite\ntriangulation\nthe problem\nof the geometrical\nmeaning of the measure of\ntime known as Universal\nTime (UT).\nAs is well known, the time of day is transmitted by radio signals broadcast\nfrom numerous\nstations\ndistributed\nall over the\nworld. Abstracting\nthe delays due to physical\ncauses in the transmitters\nand receivers\nand\ntheir antennas\nand to variations\nin the propagation velocity of light caused by atmospheric\ninfluences,\nthese time signals represent\na sequence of extremely\nregular\nintervals.\nThey\nare monitored\nby atomic clocks with great\nand long-term\nstability\n(\xc2\xb110 -l\xc2\xb0 sec over a\nperiod of months\nwith daily variations\nof\n_10 -1\' see).\nIn principle,\nthese transmitted\nsignals do\nnot represent\na time referred\nto the Earth\'s\nrotation,\nbut to a definite signal sequence.\nFor most daily and public purposes, however,\nit can be considered\ndirectly\nas the "time of\nday."\nBy means of stellar observations\nat a\ngroup of observatories\nlinked in an international\nservice, the relation\nbetween\nthese\ntime signals and time referred\nto the Earth\'s\nrotation\nis established.\nIn addition,\nthis international\nworking\ngroup\nconcerns\nitself\nwith the determination\nof the instantaneous\nposition of the pole. These figures are published in the form of preliminary\nand later\ndefinitive values.\nOne set lists the position of\nthe instantaneous\npole with\nrespect\nto a\nselected null position;\nother tables give time\ncorrections\nfor converting\nthe transmitted\nsignals to Universal\nTime 1 (UT1) and Universal Time 2 (UT2).\nThe results from the\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nvarious observations\n(58 are participating\nat\nthis time) are combined\nat the Bureau\nInternational\nde l\'Heure\n(BIH)\ninto a "mean\nobservatory"\nvalue. This eliminates\nneglected\ninfluences such as refraction\nanomalies,\nsecular polar motions,\nand irregular\nchanges\nin\nthe Earth\'s\nrotation\nstatistically,\nat least to\nsome extent.\nIt also smooths out the errors\nin the determination\nof time due to systematic\nbiases caused by the assumed\nnominal longitudes\nof the various\nobservations\nand long-term\nrefraction\ninfluences.\nUniversal\nTime 2 in this system\nrefers\nto a\nfictitious\nEarth that is practically\nindependent of periodic, chiefly seasonally\ndependent,\nchanges\nin the rate of the Earth\'s\nrotation.\nUT1 is characterized\nby the fact that it,\nlike the original\nobservation,\ncontains\nthe\nperiodic,\nseasonal\nvariations\nof the Earth\'s\nrotation\nand therefore\nrepresents\na measure\nof the instantaneous\nrate of rotation.\nHence,\nit is a more suitable\ntime for the present\npurpose,\neven though it is not uniform,\nand\nthe time interval\nso determined\ncan, as was\nindicated\nabove, be converted\ninto the corresponding\nsidereal\ninterval\nby multiplication\nwith 1.002 737 91. Since 24 hours of sidereal\ntime represents\nexactly one revolution\nof the\nearth relative to the right ascension-declination system,\nthe so-computed\nsidereal\ntime\nis proportional\nto an angle of rotation,\nthe\ngeometric\nequivalent\nof our time coordinate,\nand is represented\nin figure 7.19 in a form\nconvenient\nto the purpose,\nas sidereal\ntime\nof the null meridian\n(see also fig. 7-18a).\nBy\nintroducing\ntwo great circles to include this\nangle, one obtains\na definition\nvalid for all\ninstantaneous\npositions of the pole. One great\ncircle is the meridional\ntrace of the plane containing the instantaneous\naxis and pole of the\nEarth\'s\nrotation\nand the point of the celestial\nequator that represents\nthe equinox of the observational\nperiod.\nThe other great circle is\nthe null meridian,\nwhich is the trace of a\nplane again containing\nthe instantaneous\naxis\nof rotation\nplus an arbitrary\nbut uniquely\ndefined point of the Earth\'s\nsurface.\nThis\npoint, by international\nconvention,\nhas the\ncoordinates\nlongitude\n_to 0 and latitude _,)= 0\n=\nreferred\nto the mean position of the pole for\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nthe period\n1900-1905.\nUnfortunately,\nthis\npoint lies in the Atlantic\nOcean, and no direct\nobervations\nare possible from it. This situation is regrettable\nfrom a geodetic\nstandpoint, but conceivably\nreveals the foresight\nof the specialists\ninvolved in the extraordinarily complex and difficult problem\nof reducing astronomical\nobservations\nmade for\nthe purpose of time determination.\nThe ultimate\nrefinements\nin time determination\nare not of decisive\nimportance\nin\nthe method of geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation treated here, since the accuracy required\nin timing the instant\nof star exposures\nis at\nmost \xc2\xb13 msec. Consequently,\nUT1 furnishes\ngeometric\nsatellite triangulation\nwith a time\ncoordinate\nwhose geometric\nequivalent,\nwhen\nit is transformed\ninto a sidereal interval,\nis\ncompatible\nwith the coordinate\ntransformation (7.20) of the previous\nsection.\nIn the past the situation\ndescribed\nabove\nwas complicated\nby the fact that UT1 and\nUT2 were not referred\nto the conventional\n1900-1905\npole, but were until 1958 referred\nto a pole which dropped\nperiodically,\nand\nthereafter\nthese times had as a reference\npole\na periodically\ndisplaced\npole with a so-called\nsecular\nmotion.\nSince for all these various\npositions\nof the pole the corresponding\nnull\nmeridian\npassed through\nGreenwich,\nits intersection\nwith the conventional\nequator was\ncorrespondingly\ndisplaced.\nAs a consequence,\nfor the period\nfrom 1958 to 31 December\n1967, UT1 cannot be used as a rigorous measure for rotation.\nAfter 1 January\n1968 the\ntime pole is stationary\nand identical with the\n1900-1905 conventional\npole (CIO) as recommended\nby the International\nAstronomical\nUnion 1967.\n\nSURVEY\n\n555\n\ncorresponding\nto an instantaneous\nposition\nof the\nEarth\nto which,\ntherefore,\nthe\nphotogrammetric\nrotation\nmatrix _R,(a,o,,K)t\ncomputed\nwith\n(7.31)\nis referred\nas well.\nFrom the The principle of the photogrammetric\nmeasuring\nmethod is most conveniently\nidentified with the concept of a direction\nfixed\nwithin\na certain\ncoordinate\nsystem.\nIt is\ntherefore\nreasonable\nto expect a clear and\ncomputationally\neconomical\nsolution\nwith a\nvectorial\npresentation.\nIn this connection\nit\nshould be borne in mind that the mathematical expression\nfor a direction\nin space can be\nchanged\neither\nby rotating\nthe coordinate\nsystem to which the direction\nis referred\nor\nby making a change in direction\nwithin the\nfixed reference\nframe.\nThe latter can be accomplished\neither by rotating\nthe given vector or by adding a correction\nvector. From a\nmathematical\nstandpoint,\nrotating\nthe coordinate system and changing the direction are\nequivalent.\nHowever, in associating\nthis generally valid geometric\nconcept with a specific\nmeasuring\nprocess by forming\na mental pic-\n\nk\n\nPROGRAM\n\nture of the direction\nand the related\ncoordinate system in object space, definite advantages can be derived\nfrom such a specific\ninterpretation,\nat any rate, to explain\nthe\nmeasuring\nprocess\ngeometrically.\nOne difference between the two cited operations\nis\nthat a rotation\nof the coordinate\nsystem does\nnot affect the geometry\nexisting between the\nobject points. The concept of a linear coordinate transformation,\nincluding,\nif necessary,\ntranslations,\ntherefore\nseems meaningful.\nA\nchange in direction\nwithin a given coordinate\nsystem, on the other hand, effects a change\nof the spatial\nposition\nof this direction\nin\nobject space.\nThe designation\n"change\nin\ndirection"\nwill therefore\nbe reserved\nfor this\noperation.\nFinally,\nas simple\na representation\nas\npossible will be given of the photogrammetric\nmeasuring\nmethod,\nwhose concept rests\non\nthe principles\nof a central perspective.\nFor\nthis purpose\nit is necessary\nmerely to get a\nmental picture of a unit vector Xo assigned to\na specified\ndirection\nin object space, with\nreference\nto an arbitrary\nbut uniquely\ndefined coordinate\nsystem.\nWith the assumption that the starting\npoint of this vector\ncoincides with the center of projection\nof the\ncentral perspective\n(e.g., at the center of the\nunit sphere),\nthe photogrammetric\nbundle\nvector\np in object space, reduced\nto unit\nlength, is, geometrically\nspeaking,\nidentical\nwith the vector Xo, mentioned\nabove.\nThe\nphotogrammetric\nbundle vector p being referred to the coordinate\nsystem x, y, c of the\ncamera,\nmerely a rotation\nof the coordinate\nsystem is required to transform\nthe vector x0\ninto vector p or vice versa.\nThis step, described\nabove as a coordinate\ntransformation, represents\nthe fundamental\nanalytical\noperation\nof the photogrammetric\nmeasuring\nmethod\n(see sec. 7.4.6.2.1).\nIt remains\nnow\nonly to consider\nthose displacements\nof the\nimage from the central\nperspective\nconcept\nthat are the result\nof the physical\nphotographic\nprocess.\nBefore\nan\nadjustment\nalgorithm\nis developed\nfrom\nthis line of\nthought,\nthe more important\nmathematical\naids needed in this discussion\nwill be listed\nin logical order.\n\nNATIONAL\n\n7.4.6.2\n\nGEODETIC\n\nSURVEY\n\n563\n\nMathematical Aids\n\n7.4.6.2.1\n\nFrom\n\nVARIOUS\nMATHEMATICAL\nEXPRESSIONS\nFOR THE UNIT\nVECTOR\n\nP_x_x_z)\ni\n\nx3\n\nfigure 7.27 The procedure\nis described\nin Zurmtihl\n(1965).\nFor the two-dimensional\ncase the 2 \xc3\x97 2 covariance matrix is extended to a 3 \xc3\x97 3 matrix\nby introducing\nthe number\n1, or any other\nnumber,\nas the three-dimensional\ndiagonal\n_rm and zeros for the other additional\nentries to account for the fictitious third dimenson. an observation\ntechnique\nwas developed, designed to detect small variations in camera orientation\noccurring\nduring\nthe normal 20- to 30-rain period of observation.
