program\nare\n\nin an extended\nexplained.\n\n7.4.8\n\nConsiderations\n\nTheoretical\n\n7.4.8.1\n\nError Budget\nTriangulation\n\nAs is shown\n\nin section\n\nof\n\ntriangu-\n\nof Error\n\nGeometric\n\n7.1 and\n\nSatellite\n\nat the begin-\n\nning\nof this\nsection,\nthe\nprinciple\nof the\nmethod\nof geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation\nis\nbased\non combining\na large\nnumber\nof individual\ndirections\nto satellites\nin a threedimensional\ntriangulation.\nrections\nneeded\nat the\nangulated\nindividual\ntrail\ninto\n\nThe\nstations\n\nsatellite\nto be\n\nditri-\n\nare obtained\nby interpolating\nthe\nimages\nof the chopped\nsatellite\nthe framework\nof the star\nback-\n\nground\npresent\non the photograms.\nDirections\nto the star images\nare\n\nfirst\n\ncom-\n\nputed,\nbasically\nas functions\nof the observing\ndatum,\nthe time of observation\n(UT1),\nand\nthe\ninstantaneous-pole\ncoordinates.\nThese\ndirections\nare referred\neither\nto the astronomic\nright\nascension-declination\nsystem\nfor\na specific\nepoch\n(x system)\nor, after\nappropriate\nrotation,\nto an\nEarth-fixed\nthreedimensional\nreference\ncoordinate\nsystem\n(y\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nor z system)\nin which the observation\nstation\nlocations\nare to be triangulated\n(see sec.\n7.4.6.2).\nThe satellite\nimages\nare recorded\nin an\narbitrary\ntime sequence\nthat is, however,\ncommon for all stations\nobserving\nan event.\nThe satellite\nimages\nare then interpolated\ninto the directions\nto the stars, i.e., into the\nbackground\nof stars, and thus fixed in the\nsame reference\nsystem\nto which the star\nimages have been reduced.\nThe three-dimensional position\nof the observing\nstations\nis\nfound by assigning\nto them a location such\nthat the satellite\ndirections\nemanating\nfrom\nthe various stations lead to the determination\nof the three-dimensional\ngeometry\nof all observed satellite transits.\nIt is not necessary,\naside from the practical\nrequirements\nof the field observer,\nto know\nin advance\nthe orbit of the satellite.\nThe\npoints of the orbit serve merely as elevated\nfor intersection\nof corresponding\nrays is\nneeded to fix the positions of the observation\nsites (cf. sec. 7.4.2). As a consequent\nrequirement, such rays must satisfy the "geometric\ncondition\nof simultaneity"\nexplained\nin section 7.4.4.\nThis condition\nis automatically\nmet, _or example, if the satellite trail is fixed\nby the recording\nof a sequence\nof flashes\nemitted by the satellite.\nSince to date in practice\nnot a sufficient\nnumber\nof such flashes can be generated\nto\nreduce\nthe influence\nof scintillation\nadequately\n(cf. sec. 7.4.5), we photograph\nthe\nsatellite\nin the position\nof its orbit illuminated by the Sun. In this method the trace of\nthe orbit is chopped by means of a rotating\ndisk shutter\nin the camera (cf. sec. 7.3.2, figs.\n7.12 and 7.13) into a series of time-dependent individual\nimages.\nFor physical as well\nas technical\nreasons\nit is, however,\nimpossible to generate\nsatellite\nimages at the several observing\nstations\nthat satisfy\ninitally\nthe\ngeometric\ncondition\nof simultaneity.\nBasically,\nit therefore\nbecomes necessary\nto\nfit the bundle of directions\nto the satellite for\na particular\nevent as closely as possible to the\nsatellite orbit, which is by its nature continuous. Since only a small portion of the orbit\n\nSURVEY\n\n595\n\n(about\n1-2%)\nis involved,\nthe observed\ncurve may be considered\nas part of an elliptical orbit, obeying the Keplerian\nlaws of\nmotion, which\npredicate\nthat the satellite\ndirections\nare referred\nto an inertial\nsystem\nas approximated\nfor instance\nby the right\nascension-declination\nsystem.\nOn the other hand, a solution\nbased on\nsatellite directions\nreferred\nto an Earth-fixed\ncoordinate\nsystem\nrequires,\nbecause\nof the\nEarth\'s rotation,\nthe assumption\nof a twisted\nspace curve as a model for the satellite orbit.\nIn such a procedure,\nsatellite triangulation\nis basically subject\nto five sources of error.\nThe first source\nis the uncertainties\nassociated with the star-catalog\ndata; the second\nis the accidental\nerrors in time determination\nfor the star and satellite\nexposures.\nThe\nthird is the accidental\nerrors\nin coordinate\nmeasurement\nof the\nstar\nand\nsatellite\nimages; the fourth, the influence of shimmer\nacting as an accidental\nerror\nsource;\nand\nthe fifth, the irregular\ndistortion\nof the\nphotographic\nemulsion.\nAll these\nsources\nmust be taken into consideration.\nSuch a presentation\nof the error budget\nassumes\nfirst, that the corresponding\nsystematic errors\nare sufficiently\nsmall, and second, that the mathematical\nmodel used to\nreconstruct\nthe photographic\nprocess is sufficiently close to reality.\nFurthermore,\nthe\nphotographed\nsections of the satellite\norbit\nmust be valid in a qualitative\nsense as a tool\nfor interpolation.\nAll these assumptions\nmust\nhold within such accuracy\nlimits that the influence of the remaining\nimperfections\non the\ntriangulation\ncomputations\nremains a magnitude smaller than the propagation\nof the five\ncited basic error sources.\nObviously,\nall further\nsecondary\ncorrections, such as pole displacement\n(see end of\nsec. 7.4.4), astronomic\nand parallactic\nrefraction, satellite\nphase angle, and light travel\ntime (for all these corrections\nsee sec. 7.4.5),\ncorrespond\nto geometric-physical\nreality with\nsuch accuracy\nthat the effect of remaining\nbiases is negligibly small.\nThe rigorous\ntheoretical\ntreatment\nof\nerrors of the satellite\ntriangulation\nmethod\nleads_ even from this point of view, to a\n\n596\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nmutually\ncorrelated\nmatrix\nschematic.\nThe\nindividual plates are essentially\nuncorrelated\nwith respect to the photogrammetric\nreduction, so far as processing\nthe measured\nstar\nand satellite coordinates\nis concerned.\nHowever, for all plates introduced\ninto a satellite\ntriangulation\nsystem, only one set of reference stars, limited in number\nand distribution, is available.\nHence, not only does the same group of\nstars appear repeatedly\non the same plate as\na result of star registration\nbefore, during,\nand after the event, but also similar groups\nare recorded on a number of plates.\nIn the observations\nfor the world net, stars\nup to eighth magnitude\nand with maximum\nmean position\nerrors\nof 0\':4 were selected\nfrom the SAO star catalog.\nThus, there were\nabout 20,000 stars at our disposal (sec. 7.4).\nWith an average frequency of about 100 stars\nper plate and approximately\n3000 plates in\nthe world net, this means that each star appears, on the average,\non 15 plates.\nSince,\nstrictly\nspeaking,\nthere can result only one\npair of corrections\nfor each observed\nstar in\nthe adjustment,\nthe mathematical\nreconstructions\nof\nall\nthe\nphotogrammetric\nbundles and their orientations\nare correlated\nto such a degree that they really should be\nadjusted as a unit, even if, for lack of knowledge of existing correlations,\none accepts for\nthe star\ncoordinates\nindependent\nweight\nmatrices.\nIn the spatial triangulation\nof the observing stations the satellite directions\nare now\ncombined\nto reconstruct\nthe geometry\nof\nthe recorded satellite orbit curve. The intersection condition\nfor the rays applied in the\nprocess--either\ndirect or indirect\nby way of\nfitting to a spatial model of the orbit--contains\nadditional\norientation\ninformation,\nsimilar to the relative orientation\nin the classical photogrammetric\nrestitution\nprocess.\nBut,\nsince\nall\nphotogrammetric\nbundle\nparameters\nthat determine\ndirections\nto the\nsatellite and their orientation\nquantities\nare\ncorrelated,\nthere results a correlation\nbetween\nall recorded\nsatellite\nevents;\ni.e., the determination\nof observing\nstation\npositions\nshould, together\nwith the determination\nof\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nall observed\nsatellite\norbital\ncurves, be obtained from one common adjustment\nwith the\nuse of the covariance\nmatrix involving all reconstructed\nphotogrammetric\nbundles\nand\ntheir orientations.\nProcessing\nthe approximately\n3000 plates\navailable\nin the world net requires\nthe computation\nof nearly\n60 000\ninterpolation\nparameters.\nFor the approximately\n1400 recorded events, more than 8000 orbital parameters would have to be determined.\nA simultaneous\nadjustment\nof such a large number\nof correlated\nunknowns\nis at present,\neven\nwith the largest available\ncomputer,\nneither\neconomically\nfeasible nor, because of the required\ncomputational\naccuracy,\ncapable\nof\nrealization.\nOne has, therefore,\nto make concessions.\nFrom the error\ntheoretical\npoint of view\nprobably the most serious compromise\nis the\nnecessity\nof separately\ndetermining\nthe\nphotogrammetric\ninterpolation\nparameters\nfor each plate, since these parameters\ndetermine absolute\ndirections\nto the interpolated\nsatellite images and are therefore\nof decisive\nsignificance\nin fixing the spatial positions\ncf\nthe observation\nstations.\nIn conformance\nwith the weights\ngiven with the star data\nthere is obtained\nin this procedure\nin each\nbundle reconstruction\nadjustment,\nindependent of the number of images of the particular\nstar, a pair of corrections\nfor the star coordinates.\nOn completion\nof all the bundle\nreconstructions\nunder\nconsideration\nthere\nwill therefore\nbe for each star as many corrections\navailable\nas the number\nof times\nsuch a star was recorded\non the various\nplates.\nOn the basis of the observation\ndata\nin the world net, this averages\nout to 15\ntimes. Arguing\nfrom the concept that every\nadjustment\nrepresents\nin\nprinciple\na\nweighted\narithmetic\nmean,\nthe possibility\npresents\nitself of computing\nfor each star a\nunique set of corrections\nin the form of the\narithmetic\nmean of the individual\npairs. Care\nneed be taken only to ensure, by use of appropriate weights,\nthat the mean error of unit\nweight after adjustment\nis the same for all\nthe bundle reconstructions.\nOne could then\nadd this average\nof the corrections\nto the\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nSURVEY\n\n597\n\noriginal\nstar data and repeat the bundle reconstruction\ncomputations.\nWith an appropriate choice of weights\nfor these corrected\nstar data, these values could then be held correspondingly\nfixed in the repeated\nbundle\nreconstruction.\nThe justification\nfor such an expensive\niteration\ndepends on how close the averaged\nstar-coordinate\ncorrections\ncome to the solution from a rigorous\nadjustment.\nThe significance of such a solution hinges, therefore,\non the extent to which these "improved\nstar\ncoordinates"\nrepresent\nin their\ntotality\na\nreference\nsystem which is superior to the star\ncatalog originally\navailable.\nIn the processing of the world net the improved star coordinates for the 20 000 stars being used were\ncomputed\nso that these amended\nright ascensions and declinations\ncould be presented\nto the astronomers\nfor critical\nevaluation.\nRepetition\nof the computations\nfor bundle\n\nA similar conclusion\ncan be drawn about\nthe influence of random\nerrors of the synchronization\nprocedure\non the\nsatellite\nimages. By means of periodic control of timing (sec. 7.2.1), the instants\nof observation\nat the various\nstations\nare fixed relative\nto\neach other within\nat least _+100 _sec. The\nmost critical\nsituation\nwould arise for the\nECHO satellite, with a speed of 8 km/sec and\na minimum\ndistance\nof 1000 km, for which\n100 _sec corresponds\nto a change in direction\nof _+0\':16. With the PAGEOS\nsatellite\nused\nin the world net, because of its greater\ndistance and consequent\nslower speed, a timing\nerror of _ 100 _sec results\nin a maximal\ndirection uncertainty\nof only _+0\'.\'04. Although\nthis error\nis negligible,\na calculation\nemployed in the adjustment\ndiscussed\nlater (a\ncalculation\ndesigned\nprimarily\nto eliminate\nshimmer\nby\npolynomial\ncurve\nfitting)\nserves to adjust as well any existing random\n\ncontemplated.\nAs was mentioned\nearlier,\nthe accidental\nerrors of time designations\nfor the star and\nsatellite\nrecordings\nmust be taken into consideration.\nIn the adjustment\nfor the single\ncamera this is taken care of automatically\nby\ncarrying\ncorrections\nto the right ascensions.\nThese corrections\nbeing geometrically\nequivalent to UT1, it is necessary\nonly to compute\nweights\nfor the introduced\nright ascension\nvalues, taking into account the uncertainties\nin time associated\nwith the recorded instants\nof observation.\nFor the instrumentation\nused in the world net, this accidental\ntiming\nerror amounts\nto less than a millisecond\nso\nfar as the registration\nof the shutter\naction\nis concerned.\nSince the available\nUT1 is in itself scarcely better than _+2msec (which acts\nas a system error in the orientation\nfor the\nindividual\nplate),\nthe\nassumption\nof a\n_+3-msec overall uncertainty\nin the determination of time for the star exposures\nseems\nreasonable.\nThe inaccuracy\nof a direction\ncorresponding\nto this time uncertainty\nis\n_+0\':045, a magnitude\nconsiderably\nless than\nthe photogrammetric\nmeasuring\naccuracy\nwith the BC-4 system and the 450-mm lens,\nand hence negligibla_\n\nExisting\ncorrelations\nbetween\nthe separately reconstructed\nbundles of directions\nto\nstars are, as detailed above, neglected.\nThus,\nfor each single-camera\ncomputation,\nindividual parameters\nare determined\nfor the interpolation model, including,\nof course, the covariance matrix associated\nwith these parameters,\nwhich\nis of basic\nsignificance\nfor\nfurther evaluations.\nIn the step of the adjustment\nwhich now\nfollows, the locations\nof the observing\nstations are computed.\nTheir position in space\nis fixed by the condition\nthat the bundles of\ndirections\nto the satellite\nissuing from these\nstations\nmust lead to the geometry\nof all\nsatellite\norbital\ncurves that have been recorded.\nSince each bundle of directions\nis\nobtained basically by the interpolation\nof the\ncorresponding\nsatellite\nimages into the relevant interpolation\nmodel and since these\nmodels are now no longer correlated,\nit follows that the individual\nsatellite orbit determinations are also uneorrelated.\nThis results\nin an essential simplification\nof the data processing, since the orbit determinations\ncan be\nprocessed sequentially\nand care need be taken\nonly that their cumulative\neffect bears on the\nstation determination.\n\n598\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nThe condition\nof intersection\non which, as\nwas mentioned previously,\nthe determination\nof the geometry\nof the observed satellite\norbits was based, either directly\nor indirectly\nby way of a fit to a spatial\norbital\nmodel,\nbasically contains\nadditional\ninformation\nfor\ndetermining\nthe parameters\nof the all\ninterpolation\nmodels involved together\nwith\ntheir\nindividual\nvariance-covariance\nmatrices referred\nto above, must appear as unknowns in the adjustment.\nThe resulting\nsystem of normal condition\nof\nintersection\nof the rays: the coordinates\nof\nthe observing\nstation\nand the parameters\ndescribing\nthe geometry\nof the satellite\norbital curves.\nFrom\na conceptual\npoint of\nview, this means that the bundles of directions to a satellite\nassigned\nto a particular\nsatellite\npass must fit themselves\nas closely\nas possible in the sense of an adjustment\nto\nthe orbital curve, which is subject first of all\nto the geometric\nconsequences\nof Kepler\'s\nfirst law, according\nto which the orbit can\nbe expressed,\nin an inertial\nsystem,\nby the\nequation of an ellipse.\nFurthermore,\nthe fitting process must do\njustice\nto the dynamic\ncontent\nof Kepler\'s\nsecond law, according\nto which\nthe true\nanomaly is a function\nof time. It seems convenient in the application\nto develop the true\nanomaly\nas a series in the eccentricity\nand\nthe mean anomaly.\n_,a_icaiiy _peaking,\nune\ncan say that Kepler\'s\nfirst law accomplishes\nthe fit of the bundle\nperpendicular\nto the\ndirection of the orbital curve, and the second\nlaw accomplishes\nthe fit along the orbit curve.\nKepler\'s third law cannot be used, because,\nin the first place, the orbital\nperiod of the\nobserved\nsatellite\nis not known.\nMoreover,\nthe balloon satellite\nwith its typically\nunfavorable\nmass-ratio\nis exposed to disturbing\ninfluences such as residual atmospheric\npressure and the Sun\'s\nradiation\npressure,\nso\nthat the orbital period could yield only limited\ninformation\nin a geometrical\nsense. All computational\nschemes\nmust, furthermore,\ntake\ninto account the fact that the recorded\ntimes\nfor satellite\nimagery\nrefer to the instants\nof\nexposure,\nand these data must therefore\nbe\ncorrected\nfor light travel\ntime and geometrically\n_or Earth rotation during the light\ntravel time before they can be further\nprocessed with the application\nof the principles\nof celestial mechanics.\nThe practical\napplication\nof orbital determination\nby means of bundle fitting is faced\nwith two further\nobstacles.\nAs was stated\nat the end of section 7.4.5, a relatively\nlarge\nnumber\nof satellite\nimages is needed in the\nadjustment\nto suffi__cient!y reduce the shim-\n\nSURVEY\n\n599\n\nmer effect.\nIn the world net, the number\nof images averages\n300 per plate. Since the\ncorresponding\n300 directions\nare derived\nfrom one and the same group of interpolation\nparameters,\nthey are correlated,\nwhich means\nthat for each of the satellite\ndirection\nbundles to be introduced\ninto the fit a 600 \xc3\x97 600,\ncompletely\nfilled covariance-matrix\nmust be\ntaken into consideration.\nIf the event has\nbeen observed\nby more than two stations,\nundesirably\nlarge demands\nare very soon\nmade on the memory\ncapacity\nof the computer.\nEven more decisive is the fact that\nthe shimmer\neffect depends\non the meteorological conditions\nduring the event, which\ncan be quite different\nat the contributing\nstations.\nTo prevent this "noise" from being\naveraged\nbetween\nthe contributing\nstations\nto an event in the triangulation\nadjustment,\nthe appropriate\nweight matrices\nfor the individual direction\nbundles must be computed\nby using _ne mean stammer\ncharacteristic\nfor each station.\nThis quantity\nis, however,\nin the evaluation\nmethod\nunder discussion\nand is not as yet available.\nAs an alternative\nto the bundle-fitting\nconcept, one could also fix the satellite\norbital\ncurve by smoothing\nthe spatial\ncoordinates\nof the triangulated\nsatellite\npoints\nwith\npolynomials\nas functions\nof time\n(Wolf,\n1967).\nSuch a solution\nassumes\nthat\nthe\norbital\ncurve is designated\nby a series\nof\nshort-duration\nflashes\nemitted\nfrom\nthe\nsatellite,\nthe time sequence\nof the flashes\nbeing sufficiently\nwell known. Only then will\nimages be recorded\non the individual\nplates,\nwhich lead to the triangulation\nof the corresponding\norbital points.\nOn the other hand,\nif, as is necessary\nfor practical\nreasons\nat\nthis time, the satellite\nimages are produced\non the various\nplates by chopping\nthe trail\nof the continuously\nilluminated\nsatellite with\na rotating\ndisk shutter\ninto separate\npoints,\nthen one would first have to compute\nthe\nnecessary\nlight travel times iteratively\nwith\napproximated\nsatellite\npositions.\nIn principle, this computation\nwould give sufficient\ninformation\nto interpolate\non each photogram for the event image points that satisfy\nthe gaometric\ncondition\nof simultaneity.\n\n6OO\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nFrom\nan error-theoretical\nstandpoint,\nhowever,\nsuch interpolation\nis open to question,\nbecause\nthe position\nof the individual\nimages\nis influenced\nto a different\nand\nunknown\nextent\nby\nshimmer.\nFrom\nthe\ncomputational standpoints,\nstill another\ndisadvantage\naccrues\nto this solution,\nin that\nall the satellite\ndirections\non the\nselected\nplates\nare\ncorrelated,\nleading\nto variance-covariance\nmatrices\nwhose\nconsideration\nwould\nrequire\nan intolerable\namount\nof computer\nmemory\nspace.\nThe theoretical\nand practical\nthe\nabove\nmethod\nof solution\n\ndifficulties\nof\nare circum-\n\nvented\nby modifying\nthe approach\nand evaluating\neach plate independently\nto the greatest extent\npossible.\nThis concept\nis also valid\nfrom the standpoint of error\ntheory\nand is based on the fact\nthat\nthe measurements\nat a given\nobserving\nstation,\ni.e., the photogrammetric\nregistration of the star\nimages\nand satellite\norbit,\ntogether\nwith the relevant\nrecordings\nof time,\nare\nself-sufficient\nin the sense\nthat\nthe information\nso obtained\nis completely\nindependent\nof and not influenced\nby the fact that\nsimilar\noperations\nhave been carried\nout at\nother\nstations.\nTransforming\nthese\nmeasuring data into time-correlated\nsatellite\ndirections requires\nonly the additional\nassumption\nthat\nthe satellite\norbital\ncurve\nis by nature\ncontinuous.\nIf the geometric-dynamic\nproperties\nof the\nphotographed\nportion\nof the satellite\norbit\nas described\nabove\nare known,\nit should\nbe\npossible\nto postulate\nthe form\nof this trail\non the photogram,\nin the direction\nof the\ntrail and at right angles to it, in terms\nof the\ncentral\nperspective\nlaws,\nlight\npropagation\ntime, and the aberration\ndue to the Earth\'s\nrotation.\nThe formalization\nwould\nlead to an\ninfinite-series\norder\nterms\n\nexpansion\nin which\nhighercould be neglected.\nThe orbital\n\nprojection\ncould\nthen\nbe adjusted\nto this\ntheoretical\nmodel\nby\nfitting\nthe\nsatellite\nimages _ to it.\nAnother\npossibility,\nthe the\nphotogram\nas a function\nof time.\nA polynomial\nfit is all the more justifiable\nfrom\nthe\nstandpoint\nof error\ntheory\ninasmuch\nas consequence\nin that from an\nadjustment\npolynomial\nof the nth degree,\nonly n+l\ncomputed\nvalues\ncan be used;\notherwise,\nthe corresponding\ncovariance\nmatrix becomes singular, while the use of fewer\nvalues does not exhaust\nthe available\ninformation content completely.\nIn processing\nthe world net, polynomials\nof the sixth degree are used in smoothing\nboth x and y, so that seven fictitious\ndirections can be used in the final triangulation,\nprovided that the trace of the portion of the\n\nSURVEY\n\n601\n\nsatellite\norbit common\nwith other\nstations\nextends over the whole plate. Thus, the poly.nomials provide the adjusted\nlocation of the\nsatellite trace as a function\nof the recorded\ntime.\nThis relation\nis very useful,\nsince it\nsimplifies the application\nof the influence\nof\ntime corrections,\nsuch as clock differences\nand light propagation.\nIt is necessary,\nafter\na selected\nsatellite\norbital\ntime has been\ntransformed\nto a corresponding\ntime\nof\nregistration\non the plate, merely to compute\nfrom the relevant polynomial\nwith this transformed time the x and y coordinates\nfor the\ncorresponding\nfictitious\nplate\nimage.\nBy\nusing this procedure\non all photograms\nthat\nhave observed\na common event, a fictitious\nimage that satisfies the geometric\ncondition\nof simultaneity\nis obtained\non each photogram (see sec. 7.4.4).\nAn approximate\npreliminary triangulation\nof the relevant orbital\npoints will be needed to determine\nfor each\nregistered\norbital\nimage the variable "propagation time of light.\nIt should be noted in\n_,,,s _on,_uon\n_,,a_ an error of 3 _-_ "\napproximated\ndistance\nwill create\nan error\nof only 10 _sec in the time. Along with the\ncoefficients\nof the curve-fit polynomials,\none\nobtains the mean dispersion\nof the individual\nimages and, hence, the variance-covariance\nof the polynomial\nparameters.\nSince the fictitious satellite-image\npositions\ncorresponding to specified times are computed\nas functions of the polynomial\nparameters\n(cf. eqs.\n7.58 and 7.270),\nthe corresponding\nerror\npropagation\ncomputation\nwill produce\ntheir\nvariance-covariance\nmatrix,\nwhich displays\nrigorously\nthe correlations\namong\nthe individual satellite\nimages resulting\nfrom the\npolynomial\nsmoothing.\nIf seven such fictitious satellite\nimages are used, as, for examl_le, in the world net, a 14 \xc3\x97 14 covariance\nmatrix\nfor these points must also be computed.\nAt In order to make a valid comparison of the two solutions,\nit is first necessary\nto translate\nthe BC-4 coordinate\nsystem,\nwhich has an arbitrary\norigin, into the origin\nof the dynamic solution, the mass center, and\nto rotate the Doppler result about its z axis\nin order to make the two systems compatible\nwith respect\nto longitude.\nHowever,\nin the\ncomparison\nadjustment,\ntwo further\nrotations and a scale factor were modeled.\nThese\nadditional\nrotations\ngive an indication\nto\nwhat extent the orientations\nof the conventional, pole-referenced\nrotation\naxes differ in\nthe dynamic\nand the geometric\nsolutions.\nSimilarly, the scale factor reveals the difference in scale, which,\nas was pointed\nout\n\n618\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nbefore, is derived in the one case from the\nproduct GM and in the other from the measured terrestrial\nbaselines.\nThe seven transformation\nparameters\n(three\ntranslations,\nthree rotations,\nand a scale factor)\nwere\ncomputed\nsubject\nto a minimum\ncondition\non the sum of squares of residual coordinate\ndifferences,\n(in the following\nreferred\nto as\nxyz fit).\nThe resulting\nmean-discrepancy\nvector is\n14.4 m, a value which is influenced\nby discrepancies\nlarger than 20 in five stations,\nas\ncan be seen from the tabulation\nof the discrepancy vectors in table 7.14. observational\ndata of\nthe geometric\nent.\n7.5.4\n\nsatellite\n\nDerived Geodetic\n\ntriangulation\n\nis pres-\n\nParameters\n\nThe semimajor\naxis a and the flattening\nf\nof a reference\nellipsoid\nmay be regarded\nas\nthe basic\nparameters\nfor a geodetic\nworld\nsystem,\nits center\ncoinciding\nwith the Earth\'s\ncenter\nof mass.\nThe direction\nof the z axis,\ni.e., the Earth\'s\nrotation\naxis, is fixed by the\nconventionally\nadopted\nmean\npole\nposition\nat a specified\nepoch and the direction\nof the\nx axis through\nthe null meridian\nby an identifiable\npoint on the surface\nof the earth.\nIt\nis also possible,\nalthough\nhardly\npractical,\nto postulate\na triaxial\nellipsoid.\nWith the establishment\nof such a reference\nsystem,\nthe xyz coordinates\nof the combined\nsolution\nas given\nin table 7.15 can be transformed\ninto latitude,\nlongitude,\nand ellipsoid\nheight.\nFurthermore,\nclassical\ngeodetic\nresults referred\nto individual\ndatum\nellipsoids\ncan be transformed\nto such a world\nsystem.\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nUsing\nthe values\npresented\nfor the determination\nof these quantities,\nwe arrive\nat the\nfollowing\nresults.\nTo begin\nwith,\nthe\nstation\ncoordinates\nobtained\nin the geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation\nsolution\n(table\n7.11),\nreduced\nto sea\nlevel, were adjusted\nto a best-fitting\nellipsoid\nof revolution.\nThe significance\nof such a solution is somewhat\ndubious,\nin view of the fact\nthat\nonly\n43 stations\nfor\nwhich\nleveling\nheights\nwere obtained\nare available,\nand that\nthere\nis no a priori\nevidence\nthat\nthe mean\nof the corresponding\ngeoid heights\nis close to\nzero.\nThe result\nis shown\nin the first row of\ntable\n7.18\n[left-handed\ncoordinate\nsystem-Editor].\nThe resulting\ntranslations\n\xc2\xb1x, \xc2\xb1y,\nAz on line 1, as well as those\nshown\non lines\n2, 6, 7, and 11 for other\nsolutions,\nare not\nsignificant\nin themselves,\nbecause\nthey\ndepend\nentirely\non the approximation\nvalues\nfor the mass-centered\ncoordinates,\nintroduced\nfor\nthe\norigin\nof the\ngeometric\nsolution\n(compare\nbeginning\nof see. 7.5.1).\nOnly their\nconsistency\nin the various\nsolutions\nis of\ninterest.\nThe\n\nsecond\n\nsolution\n\nis a repetition\n\nof\n\nthe\n\nfirst,\nwith\nthe flattening\nf=1/298.250\nheld\nfixed, a value\nwhich\nis derived\nby dynamic\nsatellite-geodesy\nmethods\nand\nis now\nconsidered\nto be reliable.\nThis result\nis on the\nsecond\nline.\nFurthermore,\nellipsoid\nfits\nwere\nexecuted\nwith\nthe results\nof the combined solution\nresembling\notherwise\nthe solutions\npresented\non lines 1 and 2. These\nresults are given on lines 3 and 4, respectively.\nHere,\nas on lines\n8 and\n9, the \xc2\xb1x, \xc2\xb1y, Az\nindicate\nto what extent\nthe coordinate\norigin\nof the specific\nsolution\ndiffers\nfrom the mass\ncenter\nof the dynamic\nsolution.\nStill another\ncomputation\nwas performed\nwith\nthe combined\nsolution\nholding\nthe original\nposition\nof Anderle\'s\nmass-center\nfixed.\nIn this solu-\n\nzz,.\nzo*.\n\n,iz.\n\ntion, only the semimajor\naxis\na was determined.\nThis result\nis shown\non line 5. With\n\niii\nFIGURE\n\n7.59.--Histogram\ntwo sets of\n\nevent\n\nof differences\nmean\nerrors.\n\nbetween\n\nthe\nsame\nraw\nmaterial\nthese\nellipsoid-fit\nsolutions\nwere\nrepeated,\nincorporating\nthe\ngeoid\nheights\nas computed\nfrom\nraw\ndata\nfrom Anderle.\nThe corresponding\nresults\nare\nshown\non lines 6 to 10. On the eleventh\nline\nthe\nresult\nof the\n:ration-to-station\nleast-\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nsquares fit is shown, based on the matching\nof the positions of 37 stations as determined\nby the geometric\nand the dynamic methods.\nFrom the information\npresented\nin table\n7.18 it was concluded that a reference\nellipsoid with 1/f=298.250\nand a semimajor\naxis\nof 6 378 130 m would correspond\nbest to the\navailable information.\nTable 7.19 gives the corresponding\nlatitude, longitude\n(east),\nand ellipsoid heights\nwith\ntheir\nrespective\nstandard\ndeviations\ncomputed\nfrom the xyz coordinates\nof the\ncombined solution (table 7.15).\nIn table 7.2, section 7.3, the survey\ndata\nare given.\nA comparison\nof the results presented in table 7.19 with the results of astronomical position observations\nand the values\nof mean sea level observations,\nas given with\nthe survey data, allows the computation\nof\nplumbline\ndeflections\nand the determination\nof geoid heights.\nThe corresponding\nresults\nare tabulated\nin tables 7.20 and 7.21.\nThe \xc2\xb1x values in table 7.20 refer, in accordance with the given geographic\ncoordinates\nin table 7.19, to a system of positive east longitudes,\nwith the conventional\ndesignation:\nastro-geodetic=\xc2\xb1.\nThe A_ values represent\nabsolute position\ndeflections\nin the meridian\no_ tim station, positive to the sou_n. 1he computed _ values, positive to the east, however,\ndepend quantatively\non the chosen position of\nthe null meridian\nof the combined solution. In\norder\nto average\nthem out, an additional\nrotation\nin longitude\nwould be necessary,\nwhich would have to be added a_ a constant\nto all longitudes\ntabulated\nin table 7.19.\nSuch a correction\namounts to\ni\n\nAX=\n\n_.(XA-_o)\n_cos\n\n_cos _\n\n6_\n\n= - 07485\n\n(east\n\nlongitudes\n\npositive)\n(7.313)\n\nThe significance\nof such a correction\nis, however, impaired by the relatively\nsmall number\nof plumbline\ndeflections\navailable.\nTable 7.21 gives the geoid heights as compt!ted from\nthe combined\nsolution\n(table\n\nSURVEY\n\n621\n\n7.19) and the msl elevations\nof the survey\ndata. For comparison,\nthe geoid heights as\nobtained by Anderle from the dynamic solution are given in the second column and the\ncorresponding\ndifferences\nin the third\ncolumn, labeled aN.\nWith the exception\nof stations\n6011 (Hawaii),\n6012\n(Wake),\n6013\n(Japan),\nand\n6043 (Sombrero),\nthese A values are well\nwithin the expected level of accuracy.\nObviously, both sets of N values are also affected\nby the uncertainties\nin mean sea level for the\nvarious datums,\nto which the leveling data\nare referred.\nA comparison\nbetween the xyz coordinates\ngiven in table 7.15 and the corresponding\ninformation\ncomputed\nfrom the survey data\n(table 7.2) results in the translations\nAX, _y,\n\xc2\xb1Z. These translations\ntransform,\nstation by\nstation, the survey data into a mass-centered\nsystem.\nTable 7.22 shows these results, the stations\ngrouped in terms of specific datums.\nLarge\ndifferences\nin these translations\nfor stations\nwithin a specific datum suggest distortions\nin\nsuch a datum.\nIn column N of table 7.22, the geoid height\nused for the computation\nof the station shift\ninformation\nwas not available\nfrom the collected survey data, the corresponding\ngeoid\nheights\nfrom the combined\nsolution\n(table\n7.21) were used, indicated\nby an asterisk.\nFurthermore,\na set of station\nshift components was computed\non the basis of astronomical positions of the BC-4 stations when\nno other survey data were available.\nHere\nagain, the computations\nwere based on geoid\nheights obtained from the combined solution ;\nfurthermore,\nan ellipsoid\nwith f--1:298.25\nand an equatorial\nradius of 6 378 130 m were\nused. The resulting\n\xc2\xb1x, _y, and _z of shifts\n[left-handed\ncoordinate\nsystem--Editor]\nexpress therefore\nonly the plumbline deflections\ntabulated in table 7.20.\nFor datums for which more than one station is available,\ndatum\nshift\nparameters\nwere computed,\nallowing\nfor an additional\nscale factor and an additional\nrotation\n(in\nlongitude)\nin addition\nto the conventional\n\n622\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nthree translations.\nThese results\nare shown\nin table 7.23 [left-handed\ncoordinate\nsystem\n--Editor].\nThe smaller the indicated\ncoordinate differences,\nafter datum shift, the more\nclosely the survey result resembles\nthe relative geometry\nas determined\nby satellite\ntriangulation.\nBecause of the small number\nof stations\nbelonging to a specific datum, it was not possible to compute\nmeaningful\ndatum shifts\nallowing an adjustment\nin the spatial orientation of the rotation\naxis of the individual\ndatum ellipsoids,\nas desirable\nas such a test\nwould be from the theoretical\nstandpoint.\nSuch a complete set of datum shift parameters will be computed\nfor the NAD, when\nthe results of the satellite\ndensification\nprogram in the area of the North\nAmerican\ncontinent are completed.\n7.6\n\nSUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS\n\nThe BC-4 world net is the result\nof a\nstrictly three-dimensional\ngeometric triangulation, including a scale derived from classic\ngeodetic surface measurements\nexecuted between several\npairs\nof world net stations.\nBecause the method\nof geometric\nsatellite\ntriangulation\nis based on absolute directions\nas obtained\nby interpolating\nthe satellite\nposition into the background\nof the surrounding field of fixed stars,\nthe triangulation\nresults can at best be only as accurate\nas the\nastronomical\nsystem\nof right\nascensiondeclination\nitself.\nThis situation\nholds for\nboth the relative\naccuracy\nof the" reference\nstars and the absolute accuracy\nof the astronomical reference\nsystem in its entirety.\nThe\nphotogrammetric\ntriangulation,\nas a result\nof the high redundancy\nof data, should pro-\n\nSATELLITE\n\nPROGRAM\n\nvide a result valid to about 1 part in 2 million\nin terms of the average\nstation\nto satellite\ndistance,\nin other\nwords,\nstation\npositions\nwith an accuracy of _ 3 to _+4 m in all three\ncoordinates.\nThe statistical\ninformation\nobtained as a by-product\nof the various\ndata\nreduction\nsteps indicates\nthat the accuracy\nof the final result does not entirely fulfill the\ntheoretical\naccuracy\nexpectations.\nThe statistically\nproven\ninstability\nof the BC-4\ncamera\nsystem\nmust be considered\nas a\npossible source of a slight systematic\nerror,\nwhich in the adjustment\nalgorithm\nis unavoidably\ndistributed\nin accordance\nwith the\nminimum\nprinciple\nfor residual\nerrors.\nThe\ngood agreement\nof the photogrammetric\ntriangulation\nresult with the measured\nbaselines\naround the world indicates,\nhowever, that the\nfinal result is essentially\nfree of significant\nbias errors.\nA comparison\nbetween the result\nof the geometric\ntriangulation\nand the corresponding result obtained by dynamic satellite\ngeodesy from Doppler\ndata, as computed\nby\nthe Navy, shows excellent\nagreement\nin an\noverall\nsense,\nbut\nsignificant\ndiscrepancies in a few places on the globe. A combination of both results that respects fully the\ncovariance\nof the photogrammetrically\nderived directions\nbecomes possible by assuming a weighting\nof the dynamically\ndetermined coordinates\nin accordance\nwith a station position mean error of _+3.5 m. The only\nsignificant\ndifference\nbetween\nthe Navy-8D\ndynamically\ndetermined\nresult and the geometric triangulation\nis in terms of scale, indicating that the dynamic\nsolution is based on\na scale larger by 2 parts in a million.\nThe\ngeometric\nsolution\nsuggests\na value\nof\n6 378 130 m for the equatorial\nradius a of a\nbest-fitting\nellipsoid.\n\nNATIONAL\n\nGEODETIC\n\nSURVEY\n\n623\n\nAPPENDIX\n\nTABLE\n\nImages\nper\nsecond\n20\n10\n5\n2.5\n\nShutter\nspeed\n(100%
